MANILA, Oct. B. Two columns of the American forces south of this city
moved to-day. One, under command of General Schwan. left Binacayan for
San Nicolas. This force consists of the Thirteenth Infantry, three com
panies of the Fourteenth and two gun.* of Reilly's Battery of the Fifth Artil
lery- The other column, which Is made up of a battalion of the Fourth In
fantry, left Imus to sweep along the Zapote River. Tills movement In two
directions will do much to clean out the Insurgent strongholds in the
south. The rebel leaders in that section are making strenuous efforts to
keep the American troops from moving northward.
"WASHINGTON, Oct. S.â lt is learned to-night that the offensive move
ments inaugurated in Luzon to-day are for the purpose of preventing the In
surgents from Intrenching themselves so as to embarrass the operations of the
American troops when operations of the dry season begin. Colonel Thomas
Ward, Acting Adjutant General, received a dispatch from General Otis an
nouncing the movement and stating that the columns would operate as de
scribed. There are several strong positions held and fortified by the insur
gent*, and General Schwan will capture them under the instructions given
him. It Is not Intended to occupy the country traversed and when the troops
have driven the Insurgents away from their positions and destroyed the
fortifications they will return to their present station.

VESSELS FOR THE
BLOCKADE OF LUZON
WASHINGTON, Oct. Under orders
Issued by the Navy Department the cruis
er Brooklyn will sail from Hampton
Roads on Tuesday for Manila via Suez
Canal. The Nashville will leave San Juan
this week for the same destination by the
same route. The New Orlans will require
six days in which to make slight altera
tion?, and she will then proceed to join
Rear Admiral Watson. Reports received
from the auxiliary cruiser Badger show
that she is In need of considerable repairs,
and it may be considered advisable to
transfer her officers and crew to the
Ranger, just completed, and dispatch that
vessel to join the Asiatic squadron. Orders
have been telegraphed to the Boston Navy
Yard to expedite work on the gunboat
Bancroft, and she will be placed in com
mission without loss of time and sent- to
the far east. The gunboat Annapolis is
now at the Norfolk navy yard, and her
repairs will be pushed. The protected
cruiser Albany will be completed by Jan
uary 1, and orders will shortly be issued
for the preparation of her detail of officers
and crew, and they will be sent to Eng
land to take charge of the vessel, pro
ceeding at once to Manila. The gunboat
Machlas- has been ordered to Boston to
undergo repairs, and the Marietta may
be sent to Manila without making the
survey of the mouth of the Orinoco orig
inally ordered.
It is confidently expected that such a
strict blockade of Luzon will be maintained
by Rear Admiral Watson when all the
vessels of his command have reached
their destination that the Filipinos will
not be able to renew their supplies of
munitions of war and food. The blockade
which has been Instituted since the rebell
ion began has been moderately successful.
The last mall reports from Manila an
nounce the capture of three sailing ves
sels varying from twenty-four to seventy
three tons register, designated as the
Neustra Senora de Remedio, Carman
Venus and Campana. The seizures were
made by the Princeton off the coast of
Batagas. Luzon, and the owners and offi
cers of the ships were charged with illegal
practices In effecting clearances, In fal
sifying log books and engaging in illicit
trade at island ports in possession of the
Insurgents. The last advices received are

OPPOSES GROWING
WARLIKE SPIRIT
NEW YORK, Oct. Bolton Hall to
day gave out a statement concerning the
formation of a new organization 01
which 'he Is temporary secretary, which
is to be known as the American League.
The purpose of the American League is
to oppose the growing warlike spirit in
the United States. A meeting has ad
ready been held and a platform adopted.
which, first of all. reaffirms the doctrines
of the Declaration of Inaepen ien< â¢ and
says chat others as well as the people of

government derives its just -^ower from
the consent of ihe governed," and the
oor.ciuslon is. "We are therefore opposed
to the us.- of force in the extension of
American institutions."

are Carl' Schurz. Edward M. Shepard, W.

FOR PHILIPPINE POLICY
NEW YORK, Oct. B.â Robert Treat
Payne Jr., Democratic candidate for Gov
ernor, in an Interview said:
"President M. Kit, ley has shown his
hand. Neither Christianity nor morality
nor faithful adherence to tne principles
of our constitution, which he has taken
the oath to observe, seem to be dominant
' motives in extending our sovereignty
i over the Philippine islands. In one part
I of the archipelago a treaty has been made
with the Sultan of the Moros. where po
lygamy flourishes and slavery prevails,
j and it Is agreed to protect their Institu
tions and bar out Christian missionaries
from among his Mohammedan subjects.
The boy at the Latin school would call this
a declaration contrary to or without re
â  gard to fact. The thirteenth amendment
. to the constitution aeclares that slavery
shall not exist in the United States or any
place subject to their jurisdiction. Yet
here President McKinley ha 3 raised the
star spangled banner as an emblem to the
Mohammedan for protection to the harem
and the slave pen."

SAN FRA3N CISCO, MONDAY, OCTOBER 9, 1899.
BRITISH CAMP AT MAFEKING
AROUSED AND GUNS UNLIMBERED
Boers Move to the Border in Large Force and Great
Britain Is Rushing Preparations for
the Coming Hostilities. :
PRESIDENT KRUGER IN THE PULPIT AT PRETORIA.
While questions of state occupy th attention of Oom Paul during the week, on Sundays he preaches to his follow
ers in the Reformed Dutch Church at the capital of the republic. This picture is reproduced from a sketch in Black
and White.'
LONDON, Oct. The important i
news from South Africa comes .
from Mafeking, where twice on
Saturday the British camp was
aroused, the men stood to their
arms, guns were limbered and patrols j
were dispatched In the direction of the I
border. No hostilities have occurred as i
yet, but the enemy has moved practically
to the border, eight miles from Mafeking,
in force estimated at least 6000, comprising
five commandos. The garrisoning and
fortifying of the town are practically
completed. The streets are barricaded
and a perfect system of mines has been
laid. Two armored trains have arrived at
Mafeking, consisting of three bullet
proof cars, the first of which carries a
searchlight, while the rest of the cars
are loopholed. Each train Is complete in i
itself and carries its own provisions. Ex
periments with lyddite in the mines prove I
that it is an extremely powerful explo- .
sive.
Colonel Robert Stephenson Smith Ba- j
den-Powell, commanding the Fifth Dra- j
goon Guards, has issued a. notice that the ;
Boers who are massed on the Natal and
Bechuanaland frontiers have orders not
to fire until the British begin, and that
the town is well prepared for eventuali
ties, but as the Boers may shell the place
every endeavor should be made to safe
guard the women and children.
An armored train was inspected at Kirn
berly Saturday. A signal station has been
erected with a conning tower at the top
130 feet above the tracks. Communication
by telephone is had with a central office
and signals are given to the troops by
flag. By this means maneuvers were suc
cessfully, conducted Saturday. The troops
number 3000, including the bicycle corps.
A train was also successfully tested at
Mafeking. It patrolled to the north and
practiced firing at targets.
A dispatch from Kirnberly to the Cape j
Argus says that 3000 Boers are encamped j
midway between Boshof and Kirnberly. |
They are well armed and great religious
fervor is shown in the camp. The Boers,
however, are chaffing under the delay.
They patrol the border nightly and have
been ordered not to permit a British uni
form to approach the frontier.
A special order gas gazetted on Satur
day announcing what British residents
would be allowed to remain in the Trans
vaal and under what conditions.
Whatever may be the result of
Great Britain's controversy with the
South African republic. every de
partment of the Government to-day Is j
as busy as though actual hostilities had J
begun. The electric flash that announced i
the mobilization of the army reserves and j
the summoning of Parliament set every
wheel of the Government machinery in
motion. An hour after the gazette ap- I
peared executive orders were being dis
patched, from the War Office to every j
section of the kingdom, and the 10,000 bul- j
letins which appeared posted throughout |
the country to-day arc said to have been j
identical with the proclamation prepared
for use had the Fashoda incident re- 1
quired such a step.
At the same time Lord Salisbury and
Mr. Balfour were issuing thÂ© necessary
orders for the reassembling of Parlia- '
ment and the admiralty was concentrating !
its transports, twenty-four of which are I
now at Liverpool alone. So complete
were the preparations that 25,000 reserves I
have already individually received coupon
tickets, which contain instructions where
each man shall report for railway trans
portation to the place designated and a i
money order for three shillings for pro
visions en route.
At Woolwich to-day it was asserted that
95 per cent of the reserves would be fully
equipped within six days.' In the mean- j
time the members of the two. houses of ]
Parliament' are arranging to' return to
London, and a force of men is busily en- i
gaged in completing the improvements at j
Westminster.

STEYN HOPES FOR A
PEACEFUL SETTLEMENT
BLOEMFONTEIN, O. F. S., Oct. B.â
The correspondence between President
Steyn and, Sir Alfred Milner, British High
Commissioner in South Africa and Gover
nor of Cape Colony, has been published.
In a communication dated September 26
President Steyn pointed out that tho tone
of the British dispatches was altered and
that the Imperial Government' had de
parted from the original basis of. negotia
tions with the Transvaal. On 'his advice
the Transvaal had agreed to the holding
of an inquiry, and the Free State was
greatly disappointed that Its" efforts In
favor of a peaceful solution had not
availed. "-â  ''â â â â 
The Free State, Mr. Steyn said, was
still prepared to tender its services to pro
cure a solution of the difficulties on fair
and reasonable lines. It felt hampered,
however, by want of knowledge of the
definite object and the extent of the de
sires or demands of the Imperial Govern
ment, â¢ compliance with which the Gov
ernment considers itself entitled to Insist
upon, and the grounds upon j which such
insistence were based; and further by the
fact that notwithstanding repeated assur
ances that the Imperial Government does
not wish to interfere with the internal
affairs of the Transvaal or to disturb its
independence it has pursued a policy
justifying contrary conclusions.
The Free State, says Mr. Steyn, cannot
conceive it possible that a difference re
garding the franchise or representation
can justify the extensive- and ever-in
creasing military preparations being
made upon the borders not only of the
Transvaal but also upon those of the Free
j State, and is therefore reluctantly com
| pelled to conclude that they must be in
: tended to secure objects, a knowledge of
' which might induce the Free State to
; make representations to secure their at
! tainment.
In conclusion President Steyn says he
! trusts the Imperial Government may see
j its way clear, pending further negotia
j tions, to stop further movements for the
. increase of troops, and that he would be
glad to be favored with the views of the
, Imperial Government regarding tho pre
! else nature and scope of the concessions
or measures: which it suggests as neces
. sary or sufficient to secure satisfaction and
permanent solution of the existing differ
! ences. '. ': 'vV.;;,'.: ".

MANIFOLD USES FOR
WIRELESS TELEGRAPHY
Among Other Marvelous Achieve 3
ments Marconi Announces That
He Can Send Pictures by
the System.
"Could wireless telegraphy be applied to the sending of
pictures?"
"Certainly. Given time sufficient for the installation of
plants, 1 would guarantee to transmit through the air a picture,
perfect in outlines, of the finish between the Shamrock and
-oiumbia-" â Extract from an interview with Signor Marconi.
NEW YORK. Oct. R.â Wireless teleg
raphy is destined to play an im
portant part in every day life of
the future. This is the consensus
of expert opinion as gathered by
the Herald from many leading
electricians, including Thomas A. Edison
and Signor Marconi. Unable to supersede
telegraphy by wire or submarine cable,
since there can be no general guarantee
of secrecy for dispatches and operations
are at present limited to short distances,
the wireless system will yet occupy a field
which these other methods ot transmis
sion by electricity cannot fill.
For communication between ships at
sea, especially when in distress, and be
tween ship and the shore, to transmit
news or give warnings, wireless telegra
phy is likely to prove one of humanity's
greatest boons. When ordinary tele
graphic communication has been lost as
the result of storm, the wireless system
may be of important use as a temporary
substitute. Military and naval authori
ties hold that the new system will be an
important factor in future operations. In
directing the movements of a scattered
squadron, when ordinary signaling is im
practicable, to communicate between
forces in the field when miles apartâ
these are very obvious uses.
"He succeeded where I failed." This is
the tribute paid to Signor Marconi by
Thomas A. Edison. Edison was inter
viewed to-day at his home in Llewellan
Park, and he consented to talk about
wireless telegraphy. Edison said:
"There is certainly a great future ahead
of it. What that future may be no one
can say. It would seem as if we were
approaching the limit, but, then, so it was
thought a long time ago. Marconi has
proved that the electrical waves can be
jumped around the curvature of the earth
and this has heretofore been an insupera
ble obstacle. With this accomplished,
there Is apparently no end to the dis
tances that can be overcome. So far it
seems as if about 200 miles was the limit,
but yet even this distance may be as
nothing in the future."
- "Is the system a new one, or is It only
an adaptation of" old principles?"
"The idea is not new, and others have
been experimenting along the line. I had
patents out on this line long before we
heard of Marconi. I tried the experiment
at Menlo years ago and kept at it for a
long time, but I could not get over the
obstacle of the curvature of the earth.
and about two miles was the limit that
I could reach. Telegraphing from mov
ing trains was In line of my invention,
but I and all others ran up against ob
stacles that could not be overcome."
"What is your estimate of the practical
value of the invention?"
"Well, It will prove of great value in
a maritime direction, but I cannot say
that as at present developed it will be of
much value in land operations. The sys
tem can bo made of great practical use
in sea service by stationing vessels or
tugs a certain number of miles apart, but
near enough to each other to come within
the sphere of electrical Influence. In this
way lines of communication could be es
tablished for a thousand miles or more.
Islands could be connected in this way,
or perhaps groups of such Islands brought
Into close communication with each other.
It Is possible that some valuable use
might be made of the system on land, and
indeed some time ago in maneuvers of the
British army and fleet the wireless sys
tem worked well. Possibly the use of
aeroplane kites in connection with the
system may be found to have a practical
value. All this is yet to be developed."
"Are you working at all on this line?"
the Inventor was asked.
"No," he replied. "I gave the whole
thing up years ago and have dismissed it
from my mind. Perhaps one of these days
I may take It up again, but that is a con
tingency too remote to speak of."
Signor Marconi said to-day:
"Two years ago one of the most learned
electricians in all England said that be
yond a distance of half a mile wireless
signals could not be exchanged. At that
time I had been successful at a greater
distance, but I was then of the opinion
that the limit would be about ten miles.
Now we are operating between stations
one hundred and ten miles apart. In the
very near future I will have ready what
Is knewn as the deflector. It has already
been perfected, but for personal reasons
I have not yet given It to the public. By
means of the deflector ships will be able

"In England the lighthouses are already
using wireless telegraphy. My plan is to
have all ships equipped with signaling
machines and deflectors. It will then be
possible to send signaling currents all in
one direction instead of broadcast, as is
now done. The deflectors will be revolved
about the mast, and as the currents reach
the point from which they are sent to the
receiver an indicator will ring and the
pilot> may steer his vessel along a safe
course, being guided by signals from
shore."
Signor Marconi was asked whether he
had any knowledge of the telediagraph In
vention which is being used by the Her
ald to send and receive pictures by wire.
"Oh, yes," he said, "I have given it
some study, and it is in its way a won
der. Like wireless telegraphy, it has yet
to be brought to its fullest perfection, but
even now it is capable of great practical
results."
"Could wireless telegraphy be applied
to the sending of pictures?"
"Certainly. Given time sufficient for the
installation of plants, I would guarantee
to transmit through the air a picture per
fect in outlines of the finish between the
Shamrock and Columbia."
WASHINGTON, Oct. B.â is . under
stood that the cruiser New York and bat
tleship Texas will be equipped with in
struments and a shore station will be es
tablishced at Atlantic Highlands. Ex
periments have already been made by the
life-saving service, but the result has not
been decisive. If satisfactory, life-saving
stations and lighthouses will probably be
equipped with the instruments.
The experiments to be made by the
army and navy will be exhaustive. To
quote officials, the yacht race reports have
shown that the system is practicable for
general operation at sea. It only re
mains to prove whether it will be satis
factory for naval and military purposes.
If a success it will be adopted by the
navy, and the army in the Philippines will
be equipped with instruments. The light
house and life-saving services would also
adopt it, and merchantmen will be ad
vised to equip their ships in order that
they may communicate with shore in case
of disaster.
Naval experiments will be made under
the general direction of Rear Admiral
Farquhar, who will succeed Rear Admiral
Sampson in command of the North Atlan
tic squadron. Officers now witnessing the
operations of the system in connection
with the yacht races will conduct experi
ments at sea. Speaking of the matter to
day Admiral Bradford said:
"There is no question of its prospectve
value. I am not yet prepared, however,
to say how great that value will be. Offi
cers whO are witnessing the present trials
have not submitted their reports."
Colonel James Allen, acting chief signal
officer of the army, states that the two
automobiles recently bought will De used
in the army experiments. Small balloons
and kites will be employed and their use
may increase the distance over which a
message can be sent. He said:
"The value of wireless telegraphy in
creases with distance. Thus, if a message
can be sent fifty miles and given a value,
for instance, of two, its dispatch over
one hundred miles would make the sys
tem worth four. Should the system op
erate satisfactorily over one hundred
miles communication by wireless teleg
raphy could be maintained between Key
West and Cuba, between the islands of
the Hawaiian group and between some
of the .islands of the Philippine archi
pelago. The best results with the system
have thus far been obtained over water.
No satisfactory results have been secured
over a wooded country. Naturally we
are hopeful that the experiments will
show that the system can be operated
on land. It will then be highly valuable
in maintaining communication between
operating columns, between such columns
and their base and between the besieged
and their friends. In case the system
proves successful Instruments and the
necesary appliances will be sent to Ma
nila."
chief Moore of the Weather Bureau is
another Government official who is watch
ing the experiments with wireless teleg
raphy with much interest. Officials of the
bureau look forward to the possible use
of the invention in gathering Information
regarding the weather and sending out
forecasts and storm warnings.

BOY WHO TRIED TO
MURDER HIS PLAYMATES
Twelve- Year-Old Louis Ragio About to Be
Tried at Salinas on Several
Serious Charges.
SALINAS, Oct. B.â There is no doubt
now but what Louis Ragio.. the. .-
ear-old son of Philip Hapio r,f Santa
Rita, a small town east of â¢ this city,
Is the worst boy In this pari of thi State.
For a long time Ragio has been called
"the terror of Santa Rita," but now the
terror of Monterey County Is by far a
more befitting title.
Rag although young In years, is old
In crime, his latest offense being horse
stealing. This crime, added to that of at
tempting to murder three little mates
and various other offenses, if all are
brought to trial, will cause the lad a long
term of punishment.
On May 2 three small boys were playing
near their homes in Santa Rita. Ragio
joined them, whereupon the bids, who
had been forbidden by their parents to
r.ssoriate with him, asked that he leave,
telling the reason. Ragio insisted, and
the boys as vehemently objected, where
upon the bad boy said, "I'll fix you." and
going to his own home he secured his
father's shotgun, loaded 11 with birdshot
and approaching within about fifty feet
of the boys fired. The shot took effect In
all three, but, fortunately, the injured
ads recovered.
Rapio was captured, taken into court
and placed under heavy bonds. Ills case
has been postponed on various occasions,
and the other day it coming to the boy's
ears thai he would soon be brought into
court he attempted to jump his bail and
escape. He stole a horse and with it
reached Aromas, where he was captured.
He Is now lodged In the County Jail and
various charges will be placed against
him. The charge of having tried to take
the life of his playmates is to come up
this week.
Eouis Ragio Is a half-breed Mexican and
Indian. His father has been In numerous
Fc:apes and lias served time in State
prison. Evidently bad blood will show
up despite all attempts to keep It hidden.
JOSEPH MARTELLA
HAS DISAPPEARED
Many Believe Rancher
Was Murdered.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
POINT REYES STATION, Oct. B.â The
name of Joseph Martella, a prominent
rancher and politician of this place, has
been added to the long list of those who
suddenly disappeared. Martella has been
missing since last Monday under peculiar
circumstances, leaving his friends in
doubt whether he has gone of ' his own
accord, committed suicide or was mur
dered.
The missing rancher owns 2000 acres of
land In this vicinity and is reputed to be
very wealthy. He has always taken a
keen Interest in politics i nd far years has
attended the Democratic county conven
tions as a delegate. It is alleged that for
some time he has had a good deal of
trouble with his wife and consequently
has been In a gloomy frame of mind.
Last Monday Martella went to San Ra
fael, taking with him considerable coin,
and consulted an attorney as regards
â winding up his financial affairs. No defin
ite agreement was reached, but from thai
day to this nothing has been seen or
heard from the rancher. The affair was
reported to the, authorities to-day, and a
deputy sheriff will arrive to-morrow to
make an Investigation.
Few Incline to the suicide theory, and
fewer to the belief that Martella has
"skipped," as no disposition has been
made of his property. The authoiities
generally are favorable to the murder
theory on account' of die money the
rancher had on his person when he dis
appeared.
TRIAL OF GAMBLERS
ON THE GEORGEANNA
Criminals Who Robbed Passengers
on the Boat During the Yacht
Race Held.
NEW YORK. Oct. B.â The thirteen men
alleged to have been participants in the
riot which occurred Saturday aboard the
Philadelphia steamer Georgeanna, were
arraigned to-day before a city magistrate.
Captain C. A. Norton of Hartford.
Conn., who organised the committee of
passengers which took possession of the
steamboat and held the gang of gamb
lers and blacklegs at bay until the boat
reached her pier, was present to prose
cute the men who had stabbed at him
and threatened to throw him overboard.
With him were several other, passengers.
The prisoners were Captain Alexander
Sterlck of the Georgeanna; his pilots
Henry B. Post and Daniel Brown, and
Charles Berry, who had chartered the
boat and the other alleged gamblers, Ed
ward P. Sherman, Prank Jones, John
Wills, George Lyons, Charles Valdwln,
William Sims. William H. Cutler, Robert
Stone and James Martin.
The evidence against Berry, described
as the ringleader of the gang, showed that
Berry was a desperate character and that
he had slashed captain Norton's coat
with a knife in an effort to stab him
Berry was held In $2000 for further exam
ination on Wednesday. Sherman, charged
with splitting open the scalp of Peter Per
due, was held In $1000 ball for examination
on the same day.
Captain Norton's testimony, which was
corroborated by the other witnesses, sat
isfied the magistrate that Captain Sterick
was aware that the boat had been chart
ered by gamblers and t...it he was under
their orders. Norton testified that the
captain told him he could go only where
the men running the far... roulette, wheels
of fortune and dice games In the different
parts of tho boat wanted him. The pris
oner, however, when questioned, denied
any knowledge of the gambling. He was
fined $10, and as the witnesses all say
that the pilots had behaved themselves
during the excitement they were dis
charged. On complaint of a 'civil and me
chanical engineer, Sterick, Brown and
Post would be arrested for taking the
ship out to sea without a license.
During the examination it was testified
that the gamblers forcibly robbed pas
sengers of their watches, Jewelry and
pocketbooks and escaped before the police
boat came alongside.

LOUIS RAGIO. the Bad Boy of
Santa Rita.
RODE THE CREST
OF A TIDAL WAVE
Trying Experience of the
General Siglin.
Fecial Dispatch to The Call.
TACOMA, Oct.' B.â lt is not given to
many men to ride a terrific tidal wave
of an earthquake in a 100-ton schooner
and live to tell the story, much less is it
given to them to secure kodak pictures
of the resistless, on-rushing wall of water
as 11 sweeps down upon them, yet M. D.
Balsar of Tacoma has done both.
Balsar arrived yesterday. It was on his
return from Cook Inlet in September that
be encountered a tidal wave caused by
an Alaska earthquake. With thirty-six
others he took passage from Cook Inlet
on the schooner General Siglln. They were
about twenty-five miles off shore from
Cape Elizabeth at noon of September 9.
The day was beautiful and the surface of
the -â  a was smooth and unruffled, when
suddenly a gigantic wave, swelled up, ap
parently from beneath the vessel, and the
calm waters were Instantly lashed into a
yeast of foaming crests. While the start
led passengers were watching the heaving
waters one of them uttered a startled cry
and pointed eastward toward the shore.
Everybody looked and saw a tremendous
wave fully fifty feet high sweeping down
upon them with inconceivable rapidity.
Balsar seized his kodak and took a hasty
snap-shot at it, but the vessel had al
ready felt the influence of the wave and
pitched over on her side, thus In a meas
ure spoiling the picture.
The next Instant the schooner was lifted
and to the startled passengers it seemed
as though they had commenced a trip to
the sun. An instant later and they were
sinking again, down deep into the caver
nous Jaw of the waters. The rolling of
the ship was frightful and all the passen
gers were compelled to seek refuge In
their cabins, although the captain bravely
stood at the wheel. One moment the ves
sel would be lying on her beam ends, the
next the bowsprit would make a sudden
dive toward the bottom and the next the
taffrall would be taking a hath. Many
passengers thought their last hour had
come. Some prayed, other stoically await
ed their fate
The huge tidal wave was quickly fol
lowed by others from shoreward. Tho
Siglln pitched and tossed all the after
noon, but manged to ride them safely.
The churning of the water continued until
after sundown. 'When darkness came on
a storm sprang up and the barometer fell
two points. All sails were reefed and
through the night high seas kept running.
This continued until 11 o'clock the next
day. when the wind subsided and the best
kind of weather was thereafter experi
enced to Puget Sound. Though knowing
nothing of the earthquake on shore the
passengers believed the tidal wave had
been caused by seismic disturbances.

RAIDED BY POLICE

Game of Poker.
BOSTON, Oct. The Oxford Club, j
formerly the Suffolk Club, a well-known j
social organization, wan raided at mid
night Saturday and thirty-six of Boston's
wealthiest and most prominent citizens
were arrested on the charges of gambling
and being present where gambling was In ;
progress. The majority of these men
are millionaires, and there Is the greatest
excitement to-day because of the strenu
ous efforts being made to keep the affair I
from becoming public.
A steep game of poker was In progress ;
and $4000 in chips were seized. â¢ The men j
arrested were taken to the station and
gave assumed names. They depostied '
cash ball and were all released. None of
them will appear in court to-morrow, i
their bail will be forfeited and the case j
will be hushed up completely, as there
seems no doubt that sufficient influence
can be brought to bear to prevent prose
cution by default. If this cannot be done
there will be an exodus of leading Bos
tonians for fall pleasure resorts. The cli
max to the sensation, however, is the an
nouncement that a former prominent and
wealthy citizen of Boston committed sui
cide a few days ago because he lost the
last remnant of his fortune, $7000, In this
same club only a few days ago.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO, Oct. B.â The Tribune
says: Word came to local Demo
cratic circles to-day that if Chair
man Jones of the Democratic Na
tional Committee could have his
way Chicago would not be the
headquarters of that committee
pending or after th.- national con
tention. The report is that Chair
man Jones wants to take the head
quarters to Washington and the
general idea is that he will, do as
he pleases about It. The reason
given for selecting Washington for
the home of the committee is that
its work can be more easily accom
plished at the nation's capital.
NEW YORK. Oct. B.â United States
Senator James K. Jones of Arkansas,
chairman of the National Democratic
Committee, and wife were passengers on
the Cunard liner Lucania, that reached
New York City to-day. They have been
traveling in England, Ireland, Scotland,
Switzerland and Wales for several
months. Senator Jones said he had been
greatly improved by his travels, and felt
able to go into the work necessary in get
ting ready for the Pres.dentlal campaign.
Senator Jones, when asked to give his
views on the political 'situation, said that
owing to his long absence abroad he was
not as fully informed as to political con
ditions as he hoped to be in a lew weeks.
When asked his opinion as to the proba
bility of the Democratic nomination for
the Presidency going to W. J. Bryan he
said: â¢ '"â ' '- '--â -â 
"There is no doubt that Bryan will re
ceive the Democratic nomination for Pres
ident," and added, "has anybody any
doubt on that question? Bryan is sure to
j win." â â 'â¢'â â 'â  ' : ','''-''
On the attitude of Richard Croker as to
the nomination of Bryan for President,
Senator Jones had little to say, merely re
plying to a direct question: "Croker is al
ready in line."
As to the possible outcome of the na
tional campaign of 1900, Senator Jones said
he believed that the free silver idea would
have a greater following than in the cam
paign of 1896. He based this assertion on
the tact that the Democratic party would
receive support in 1900 from a new source.
"In 1896," he said, "there were many
Republicans and others who believed that
when the Republican party declared for
bimetallism the declaration was made in
all sincerity. These men now doubt the
sincerity of that plank of the Republican
platform."
When asked what effect he thought the
I apparent prosperity in the Western States
would have on Democratic Issues, Sen
ator Jones said:
"Yes, I know they say at Washington
; that the country is in a highly prosperous
\ condition, and perhaps that is correct
from certain standpoints. But I cannot
Bee that there Is any great material pros
perity among the agricultural and simi
lar classes. It does not appear to have
affected the farmer, nor has it done much
as yet for the laboring man in general.
Cotton and wheat have not made any ad
i vance to speak of in price. That argu
ment can be readily answered when the
time comes. Of course, there has been
; an increase in the output of gold, and
that Is bound to affect politics In some de
: gree, greater or less. But that is only
! a proof of the arguments advanced by us
in 1896, that what the country needed was
a greater volume of money in circulation."
When asked what effect he thought the
alleged prosperity would have on the vot
ers- in general, he said, in emphatic tones:
I "It will affect only those men who are
very narrow in their political and social
views. That voter It will influence, but
; In reality It will be an argument in favor
of our party."
He said that It was his opinion that the
opposing Presidential, candidate in 1900
would be President McKlnley. The Demo
cratic platform, he declared, would be
launched In that year on comparatively
the same basis and contain essentially the
same planks as In 1896. The party would
i declare for free silver at the same ratio,
j and he thought about the only addition
I to or change in the party platform of any
; moment would be a plank relative to antl
i Imperialism. That, he asserted, would un
i doubtedly be one of the Important issues
;In the campaign. He refused to discuss
, the matter any more than to say, with a
I wave of the hand, "Everybody knows
! what Imperialism means. The question
1 under discussion means Just the reverse."
During the afternoon and evening sev-
I eral men prominent In public life called
;on Senator Jones. Among them was
George Frederick Williams of Massachu
setts, and it Is presumed there was a con
ference over political matters and plans
! for the coming campaign.
STRUCK A YOUNG
LADY IN THE FACE
De Los Noble Roughly Handled by
an Infuriated Mob in
Sausalito.
SAUSALITO, Oct. 8.-De Los Noble, a
member of the local brass band, was
nearly beaten to death to-day by a crowd
of Infuriated citizens because of a
cowardly and brutal assault made by him
on a respectable young lady. He was
sneaked out of the crowd by several of
his friends, who succeeded In securing his
release by strategy and hurried him
away in time to save his life.
Noble had just come off the steamer
San Rafael, which had been on an ex
cursion around the bay, when he met
Miss Vlera. the sister of Fireman Manuel
Vlera, and struck her a powerful blow
In the face, knocking the girl to the
floor. Cornelius Mahar, the nearest wit
ness, promptly felled Noble, and a crowd
rushed In with shouts of "kill him,"
"lynch the coward," and buffeted Noble
around for several minutes before he was
torn away and carried off in a bloody
and semi-conscious condition by a few
of the cooler heads. Manuel Vlera tried
to brain Noble, but was restralnd.
It Is alleged by her relatives that
Noble's assault was due to the young
lady refusing to keep company with him
longer for good reasons. Noble was per
fectly sober when he committed the deed.
More trouble is expected over the affair.

astlc reception. The regiment arrived in

WILL DISCUSS
WORLD'S TRADE
International Congress
to Meet.
MANY NATIONS REPRESENTED
Â«
DIPLOMATIC CORPS AT WASH
INGTON TO ATTEND.
â¦
Assistant Secretary of State Will Be
the Presiding Officer and Dele
gates From Manila Will
Speak.
â 
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PHILADELPHIA. Oct. 8.--The Inter
national Congress, composed of delegates i
from most of the civilized governments
of the world and representatives from the I
most Important Chambers of Commerce I
of all trade centers, will convene In the !
auditorium of the National Export Expo- i
sition building here on Thursday next. |
Assistant Secretary of State David J. j
Hill will preside at the opening cere-]
monies, whit will begin at half-past 2|
o'clock. Mr. Hill will deliver an address
of welcome to the foreign delegates. The
full diplomatic corps at Washington will
be present to give eclat to the occasion,
and several members of the Cabinet will
accompany Mr. Hill and take part In the
day's exercises. The address of the as
sistant Secretary wiil be followed by a
speech by Mayor Ashbridge of this city,
who will welcome the members of the
congress to this city and extend to them
the courtesies of the municipality. In the
morning, prior to the assembling of the
congress, a reception will be held at tne
City Hall to the delegates and dignitaries
gathered to participate In the opening
exercises, which will be of an elaborate
character and be attended by the leading
citizens of Philadelphia. At the audito
rium, upon the conclusion of Mayor Ash
bridge's address, Mr. Charles H. Cramp,
president of the Philadelphia Commercial
Museum, will formally open the proceed
ings in an address explaining the nurpose
and scope of the congress. His address
will be followed by a speech by Director
W. P. Wilson, who will enter into a gen
eral announcement of the details of the
future meetings of the international gath- i
ering. President McKlnley, who will be i
detained at Chicago on account of his
previous engagement in that city, will.
however, personally participate in the i
opening of the congress by sending im- j
mediately upon the conclusion of the
other proceedings of the day a message i
announcing the sympathy of the United
States in the movement and extending on
behalf of the Government from Its ex
ecutive head a cordial greeting to the
delegates.
Curiously enough, among the delegates
at the opening of the congress will be a
large number of representatives from
Spain, and one of the most interesting
figures among the delegates will be the
president of the Chamber of Commerce
of Manila. The congress will enter Into
a general discussion of the world's trade
with a view of making suggestions for
the regulation of foreign commerce in
such a way as to provide for its greatest
development consistent with native in
terest. The following general scope of
the discussions will convey a fair idea
of the purposes of the convention:
Foreign trade In general, Including
questions of the establishment for inter
national arbitration from a commercial
standpoint and the establishment of in
ternational boards of arbitration for the
settlement of 'trade disputes. The ad-'
vantages of an extension of International ;
post facilities. The discussion of the |
interoceanic canal projects and the ad- i
vantage 10. all nations of an Isthmian
canal. International banking and cur
rency, including International banking In
Its relation to trade and the need of in- I
ternational laws concerning bankruptcy,
patents and trade, marks. A discussion
.of the new spheres of influence of the
United States suggested by the results i
of our recent war with Spain. Also in
quiries into the future of the British
West Indies and the application of the
Monroe doctrine to American republics.
The question of an open door policy In
China, of the South African situation, of
tariff policies, of shipping subsidies, of
trade prospects and the commercial out
look for the coming century will be the
features of the meetings.
The deliberations of the congress will
be In English, but no modern languages '
will be excluded. The congress will be j
absolutely disinterested, and the dis- I
cussions will be entirely free and un- j
checked.

SAN RAFAEL, Oct. B.â The funeral of
Attorney Hepburn Wllkins, which was of
a strictly private nature. In accordance
with family precedent, occurred this aft
ernoon at 1:30 o'clock from the late resi
dence of the deceased on Fifth avenue.
The sermon was preached by Rev. Dr.
Wyllis Hall, the local Episcopal minister.
Those who officiated as pallbearers
were: Judge Henshaw, Judge Morrow,
Judge Angellotti. A. W. Foster, John F.
Boyd and James Tunstead.
Out of respect to the memory of the de
ceased lawyer many flags were displayed
in town at half mast. At a meeting of the
San Rafael Bar Association, held last Fri
day in the chambers of Judge Angellotti.
a resolution of respect to the memory of
the deceased and of condolence to his rel-
i atives was adopted and will be presented
j in the Superior Court to-morrow by ex-
Judge Marion, after which court will be
j adjourned for a day.
COVERED WITH A PISTOL,
JOCKEY WINS THE RACE
Backers of a Mare Take Effective Steps
to Prevent the Boy From Throw
ing the Contest,
GRIDLEY Cal., Oct. B.â With a cocked .revolver pointed straight at his
head and following his every move. Jockey Norman Breedin literally ' rode
for his life" to-day, and on George Brown's mare Flora B passed under -the
wire a winner in one of the most sensational horse races probably ever wit
nessed on a track. : : IV a ii". Â»
The revolver which so effectually aided In the determination of the race
was held by one of the backers of the mare. Shortly before the horses went
to the post'lt was rumored that Breedin had been paid to throw the race. One
of the men who had bet on Flora B, just as the boy got into the saddle,
significantly informed him that he had better ride to win. Another man, who
also had placed his money on the mare, took his position at the turn where,
as Breedin flashed by, he displayed a gun. As the jockey came into the
stretch he was covered by a revolver, pointed by a third backer of Flora B.
Immediately the boy went to work on his mount with whip and heels with
such good effect that the mare passed under the wire well in the lead of
the two other horses that took part In the running.
About $600 changed hands on the race.
STATE SWEPT
BY A HOT WAVE
No Damage to Crops Is
Anticipated.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, Oct. B.â Sacramento to
day received its share of the hot wave
that rolled over the State. But the heat
was not distressing by any means. The
mercury rose to 94 degrees, but a brisk
breeze prevailed from the north, and the
atmosphere was so dry as not to be at all
oppressive. The wind was probably not
hot enough to injure fruit. To-night the
wind is cool and the weather delightful.
LOS ANGELES, Oct. B.â To-day has
been one of the hottest days ever experi
enced here at this season. The thermom
eter registered 100 degrees at 12 o'clock.
High winds have prevailed in the desert
region of Southern California. The heat
will do damage to crops.
SAN DIEGO. Oct. Bâ This was the
warmest October day In San Diego for |
twenty-seven years. At 3:15 this after
noon the mercury registered 92 degrees.
At 5 o'clock it had fallen to 82.
SAN JOSE, Oct. The hottest weather
of the season was that experienced to
day. The thermometer reached 94 and 9o i
in the sha^e. No damage will result to
the grapes unless the intense heat con
tinues for several days. The heat was
probably made more intense by the big i
forest fires in the mountains and the j
smoke.
MONTEREY. Oct. B.â To-day has been
one of the hottest ever known in this vi- I
cinity, the ,mercury registering as high
as SI degrees in the shade at noon. In
Corrale de Tarra and outlying districts
the heat was considerably greater than
here and in Pacific Grove, owing to the
forest fires in the adjacent mountains.
SONOMA, Oct. To-day has been the
hottest ever known In this valley. Ther- '
mometers in business houses and private
residences registered this afternoon be
tween 108 and 115 in the shade. The hot
wave struck the valley last night, and so
oppressive was me atmosphere that
breathing became difficult. If this heat
continues much damage will result to the
grape crop. Already many vineyards have ;
suffered from the effects of sunburn. .
STOCKTON, Oct. B.â A hot wave was
experienced here to-day. The thermom
eter registered 93. '
DAWSON DESERTED
FOR CAPE NOME
Tales of Gold Cause a Stampede
From the Klondike
Capital.
VICTORIA, B. C, Oct. B.â News was
brought by the steamer Tees, which ar
rived this evening from Lynn canal with
120 passengers and $50,000 in gold, that a
stampede is now on in earnest from Daw
son to Cape Nome. When the miners who
arrived by the Tees left the Klondike cap
ital river steamers were being left with
out crews because of the stampede, the
seamen deserting to join the great crowd
hurrying down the river to the new El
Dorado. â¢ ~.
On two of the Canadian Development
Company's steamers the captains were
obliged to call on the mounted police to
hold their crews until they were ready to
sail for the White Horse. Both vessels
made a long trip up the river, one occupy
ing eleven and the other fourteen days.
As the wood piles were deserted the crews
were obliged to go ashore and cut wood
to fuel up at every landing.
What caused the rush toward Nome
was the arrival of several Dawsonians
who had gone down In the rush of last
July with considerable gold, which they
had washed from the beach at Nome.
When they exhibited the gold at Dawson
there was considerable excitement, as the
stories that had been received there from
Nome were conflicting. That night a
stampede began, and when those who ar
rived to-night left the rush was still on.
According to the miners from Dawson,
there has been a. wholesale. blanketing of
promising gold territory in' the Klondike
by the Government. Miners have been or
dered off 1060 miles of territory, and this
exclusive of the reservations of the Gov
ernment at El Dorado and Bonanza.
It seems that immediately upon the ap
plication for ground for hydraulic pur
poses the ground is closed for. entry un
der the placer regulations, and under the
guise that the land Is to be taken up by
hydraulic concerns the Government has
taken a large amount of ground from the
miners.
Navigation will close next week on tho
upper river, and the steamers will go Into
winter quarters near White Horse.
There was ojily one more vessel to come
up when the Tees left. When about eight
hours from Skaguay the Tees struck on
Eldred rock. She was floated about ten
hours later. Her hull is damaged and she
will be hauled out.*
Fears are entertained for the safety of
the steamer Princess Louise, plying be

ENGAGEMENT DENIED.
Prince Georges and Princess Victoria
Not to Wed.
LONDON", Oct. B.â ln view of the per
sistent rumor circulated of the betrothal
of Prince George of Greece and the Prin
cess Victoria of Wales, it was announced
to-day that there was not the slightest
foundation for the report, the relationship
existing between the Prince and Princess
being sufficient refutation, as the laws of
the Greek church forbid the marriage of
'first cousins.

e Franc?sco 8 . 21 CaUfornla Â£ bel â¢ *Â»Â«Â».WILL SAIL EVERY DAY
AFTER NEXT THURSDAY
An Agreement by Which the Shamrock and, Colum
bia Are to Compete Until the Question of Inter
national Yachting Supremacy Is Decided*
NEW YORK, Oct. 6.â By a mutual
agreement between the representa
tives of the New York Yacht Club
and the Royal Ulster Yacht Club,
the Columbia and Shamrock will
sail every day after next Thursday until
the question of international yachting su
premacy is established.
The request for such an arrangement
came from Sir Thomas Lipton, owner of
the challenger. Sir Thomas wanted the
races to be sailed daily after next Tues
day, but the members of the New York
Yacht Club refused to assent to such an
arrangement.
The meeting at which the arrangement
for a change in the dates was reached
was held at the New York Yacht Club to
day. There were present R. Sharman
Crawford and H. McGlll Downey, repre
senting the Royal Yacht Club, and Lewis
Cass Ledyard, J. V. S. Oddie, F. W.
Ilurst. Edward M. Brown and H. F. Lip
pltt. representing the New York Yacht
Club. At the conclusion of the meeting
tho following agreement was drawn up
and given out for publication:
"The foregoing agreement (the challenge
agreement) is further modified by mutual
consent as follows:
"Strike out the clause beginning 'the
first ..ice shall be sailed on Tuesday,
October 3, 1599.* and substitute the follow
ing: 'The i ices shall be sailed on the fol
lowing dates until the series be com
pleted:
" 'October 10, 1599: October 12. and on
each following day except Sunday; pro
vided, however, that immediately on the
conclusion of the race on October 12, and
of each subsequent race, the regatta com
mittee shall Inquire of each contestant
whether he is willing to start the next
day, and should both contestants answer
in the negative one day shall intervene
before starting the next race. Sunday
shall not be counted as such intervening
day.' "
The agreement was signed by the mem
bers of the committees representing both
yacht clubs interested.
At the conclusion of the meeting Secre
tary Oddie said:
"In case the boats sail on the day fol
lowing a race the committee boat will dis
play the international code letter 'L.' of
blue and yeliOW squares. If there is to
be no race the letter 'M,' blue with diag
onal white stripes, will be displayed from
the committee boat. The club suggests
that each yacht and boat present take up
and display the signal given by the com
mittee boat.
"There was no friction In reach- the
agreement as to the change of dates. Sir
Thomas Lipton at first asked that the
races be started daily after Tuesday. The
club decided that this would hardly be
prudent. In the first place, it would be
entirely unfair to the public. Thousands
of persons have made arrangements to at
tend the races on a certain day. Hun
dreds of excursions have been arranged
for, and to make a change In the racing
dates on so short a notice would not be
right to the public.
"The club is deeply grateful for the
manner In which the public has been act
ire at th( races. There has been no in
terference with the boats whatever. The
club Is grateful to the public for this, and
It would not be right to make such a
sudden change In racing days. We are
endeavoring to please the public as well
as the visitors."
LIPTON'S LADS EXPECT
TO CAPTURE THE CUP
NEW YORK. Oct. B.â Shamrock stock
has ken another rise. There is a feel
ing about town to-night that the historic
cup is In greater danger than ever beore.
The more yesterday's performance Is con
sidered and discussed, the less encourag
ing it seems to the American yachtsmen.
The Shamrock is a wonder in light
weather. That is settled beyond a doubt
by the three lays of drifting. She has
surprised her own people and has given
the. other people a great shock. She
handles splendidly and has men on board
who know how to do the handling. The
feeling of the American yachtsmen that
the challenger was entering the races un
prepared and untried has disappeared.
They have seen her fit for the struggle,
and they know that she Is ready to do her
best. In response to an inquiry made this
nfternoon as to what was being done by
way of preparation, her managers said:
"Nothing. There she Is, ready for the
gun."
Each side is confident, but it must be
admitted that the backers of the green
boat are, showing the most hope. They
say they know what she can do in heavy
weather. It only required three days of
drift to convince them that she was" just
as good In light air. The whole Shamrock
contingent is happy and there is no one to
begrudge them their pleasant moments.
The Columbia people are not unhappy by ;
any means. On the contrary, they still
confidently assert that, of course, the cup
stays. But they have been shocked and ;
they have been shocked in a most de- 1
clslve manner. They know there will be i
no walkover. They know more than this, !

WILL REMAIN IN
ACTIVE SERVICE
Admiral Dewey Not to
Seek Retirement.
Special Dispatch to The Call
CALL HEADQUARTERS, WELLING
TON HOTEL, WASHINGTON. Oct. B.â
Admiral Dewey, has determined to remain
on the active list of the United States
navy. Reports have been current since
the admiral's return that he would apply
for retirement either on December 26 next,
when he will reach the usual retiring ago
of 62 years, or immediately thereafter.
Information recently obtained shows that
the admiral Intends to stay in active serv
ice. Were he to be retired he would suf
fer a Loss of $1500 a year, the allowance he
now receives for quarters, and the serv
ices of a naval aid and secretary. It is
the expectation of the department that
the admiral will remain on duty with the
Philippine Commission during the winter
and that Congress will call on him fre
quently to give advice concerning the
Philippines as well as other matters.
RETURNS THANKS FOR
DEWEY'S SAFE RETURN
WASHINGTON, Oct. B.â Admiral Dewey
to-day attended , divine services at St.
John's Episcopal Church, where the Epis
copal ceremony of prayer and thanksgiv
ing for the crown of victory our arms
and the safe return from sea, was con
ducted. The admiral and the church of
ficials advised giving no notice in advance
of this service in order to avoid the cu
rious crowd that would flock to the
church. The rector in his sermon made
no allusion to the admiral, but impres
sively delivered the two formal prayers
! but they are not telling what It Is. Leav
| ing entirely out of the question American
I pride in the American boat, all true
I sportsmen are rejoicing. Here is a con
; test worth having. Here Is a yacht race
i that Is worth all the money that has been
! spent, all the time and' care that have
: been given. It is a great struggle, and no
man can name the winner. This is the
feeling in New YorK to-day, and this is a
gain for the Shamrock side, for did she
j not start the week a bad second?
The betting is still slightly In the Co
-1 lumbia's favor, but this is net based on
the judgment of yachting experts. There
is more Columbia money to be had be
cause of local pride and this Is the influ
| encing factor in the matter of odds.
Both yacals are still at the Hook to
! day. It was a day of rest on the racers,
! Mr. Iselln and his friends coming to
! town, leaving Captain Charlie Barr on
! guard.
Sir Thomas Lipton and his friends are
near their boat on the Erin, and there
' they will remain until the last race Is
| over. Sir Thomas said to-day that un-
I der no conditions would he leave the
i Horseshoe until the contests were over,
j lit seemed very much worried about the
I 11,' ness of Mr. Fife, and he Insists that the
| Shamrock is under great disadvantage in
; not having him on board.
The opinion was quite general to-day
that there was something in yacht rac
ing by which a line could be drawn on
the merits of the boats. There was wind
enough for a couple of hours to furnish
! a test, the first real test of the week, and
| while it lasted the boats were under equal
j conditions and the Shamrock did the bet
ter work. That is the reason for the rise
lin the Shamrock stock to-day. She is
j good In a run dead before the wind. She
jis good in a beat to windward. What she
j will do in a reach Is undecided, but the
impressions are against her on this point
of sailing.
Inhere was another high northeaster to
day and the Sandy Hook waters were
| churned up In fine style. On Tuesday the
! struggle will be resumed.
Captain Barr spent the best part of his
day on board the Columbia. Mr. Iselin
left Saturday night with his entire party
of Corinthians, and when the captain
arose this morning it was to the task of
looking after a number of things which
Mr. Iselin himself ordinarily attends *o.
All the Columbia's sails were unbent on
j Saturday night, but at that time it was
I expected that one of the morning tasks
i would be to set them again. The Sun
! day trial has always been a feature up
ito this time. The state of weather to-day
j spoiled all hope along that line and the
I men did not seem to mind the priva-
I tion.
Stripped of her canvass the defender
: looked very bare. She was headed into
the gale, or as much of it as got Inside
the Hook, and the man forward on watch
kept under the lee as well as he could.
Captain Earr spent most of his time
below. Altogether the day was a dreary
one. There was nothing left for the men
to do but to 101 lon the tender and wish
for a breeze on Tuesday. Last Sunday
i they had plenty of visitors who cheered
; the boat. These crafts were missing to
day. The Sandy Hook boat came along-
side and those on board saluted the de
fender. The Hook was deserted to-day
except for the newspaper boats. In spite
of all the past fizzles the sailor men on
the Columbia still seem confident of suc
cess. The presence of the Shamrock near
by simply makes them more firm In their
belief of victory when a real trial comes.
The race of Saturday operated as a tonic
on them all. Up to that time they had
heard so much about their skipper being
outgeneraled that some of them may have
begun, perhaps, to fear that there might
be something In t. The Saturday race,
to their mind, however, showed them a
different phase and every one on board
felt that they would show the English
skippers yet what was possible. The men
had no other comment to make on the
race. The policy of those in charge of the
defender Is not to talk at all, and the men
follow the implied instructions to the let
ter.
The programme of Monday Is one of
rest. The men will do some little work in
the line of making surer, but It is not ex
pected that the Columbia will go out to
sea at all, and the energy will be re
served for Tuesday's race.
Sir Thomas Lipton spent the greater
part of to-day on board the old schooner
yacht America as the guest of Lieutenant
Colonel Butler Ames. He rose early not
withstanding the fatigue of yesterday's
race, and after breakfast spent a couple
of hours reading the newspapers. Then he
went on board the America in one of the
Erin's launches In company with Sir
Henry Burdette, Right Hon. W. J. Pierce
and Mrs. Pierce, Charles Robsell, Dr.
Mackie, Mr. J. F. Young and Sailmaker
Ratsey. I
As soon as the party was safely on
board the America left her anchorage and
under a reefed mainsail, foresail, jib and
staysail knocked about the Horseshoe for
a couple of hours. The stanch old boat
was sailing at her best In the stiff breeze
and the party enjoyed the outing very
much.
Mr. Ratsey was glad to get on board the
I In the ritual at the request of the ad

great deep this worthy servant who now
desires to return his thanks unto to thee
thy holy church."
The admiral spent the afternoon In
making a few calls and this evening took
supper with some old Washington friends.
Late to-night the admiral, accompanied
by Lieutenants Brumby and Caldwell and
the admiral's son, left for Vermont.
MILLIONAIRE HARDWARE
DEALER PASSES AWAY
George Trltch, Owner of the Largest
Establishment in the State of
Colorado, Is Dead.
DENVER, Oct. B.â George Trltch, the
millionaire hardware dealer of this city
and owner of the largest establishment of
Its kind In Colorado, died hero this after
noon of kidney complaint, aged 70 years.
He leaves a widow and five children. Mr.
Trltch came to Colorado in 1860. He was
born In Baden, Germany, in 1829, and was
brought to this country by his parents the
same year. They located at Chillicothe,
Ohio, where Trltch learned the tinner's
trade. He was married in Pittsburg and
came west, stopping a short time in lowa,
and arriving in Denver in May, 1860. He
immediately opened a shop and plied his
trade as tinner, subsequently putting In a
line of hardware. His business grew rap-
Idly, necessitating the changing of busi
ness quarters several times, and at the
time of his death he was president of the
Trltch Hardware Company, the largest
Concern of its kind in Colorado, and one
of the largest in the West.

RIVER MYSTERY IN
NEW YORK CITY
Dismembered Body of a
Woman Found.
Special Dispatch to The Ca.lL
â  NEW TORK, Oct. B.â Several parts of
the dismembered body of a woman are
at the Morgue, and the police are investi
gating another river mystery.
In the North River, at the foot of West
Seventeenth street, a man fishing last
night hauled up on his hook a bundle con-
talnlng the trunk of a woman's body.
Early In the morning the left thigh of
a woman, wrapped in several newspapers,
had been found In front of 16 West Seven-
teenth street. It was found that the por
tion of the thigh fitted the dismembered
trunk. Doctors who have examined the
parts are of the opinion that they are
those of a woman probably less than mid
dle age. The newspapers in which the
thigh was wrapped included one of the
date of October 5, showing that the body
had been dismembered on that date or
subsequent to it. There were several
marks on the papers, but the nature of
these the police will not disclose. Several
cuts on the body indicate It had been
hacked. The driver of a furniture van is
suspected and the police are hunting for
him. ''r'ZL.

PREPARING FOR CHICAGO
CORNERSTONE LAYING
Prominent Men From All Parts of the
Country and Many Foreign Rep
resentatives Present.
CHICAGO, Oct Eight States and
Territories are represented by their Gov
ernors at the fall festival. Among them
the T. T. Gear, Oregon; D. P. Richards.
Wyoming; M. A. Otero, New Mexico, and
N. O. Murphy, Arizona.
The busiest of the various reception
rooms to-day was that at the Victoria
Hotel, where a dozen Congressmen,
among them David G. Browne of Mon
tana, were received by Governor Tanner.
Vice President Mariscal of the Mexican
republic Is suffering from a severe attack
of tonsilltls; which has reduced his voice
to a whisper, and he may be compelled to
delegate his place at the speaker's table
at the Auditorium fcanquet to-morrow
night to Don Manuel Azplroz, Mexican
Ambassador to the United States.
The crowds again jammed the streets
to-day, though they were massed prin
cipally In the illuminated court of honor.
The police to-day decided that the streets
In the Immediate vicinity of the Post
office, where the conner-stone is to be
laid to-morrow by the President, shall be
kept absolutely free of pedestrians and
vehicles. People will not be allowed to
use the sidewalks unless upon impera.
tive business. This is the most stringent
order ever given for the control of a
holiday crowd.

ber Destroyed.
Special IMspatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE. Oct. B.â A forest fire that
for a week has been threatening property
1 in the Santa Cruz mountains swept over
j the country with great fury to-day as the
! result of the springing up of a high wind.
A large acreage of vineyards were swept
over and destroyed and many houses,
barns and tanks burned near Wrights
! Station. Among those who lost buildings
1 and other property were J. McCracken, E.
i A. Adams. Clyde McKinney, C. 11. Allen.
i John Carr and E. C. Yocco. The big
winery of E. Meyers was saved after hard
fighting by a number of men. It is es
, timated that property to the value of over
! $100,000 was destroyed upon which there is
considerable Insurance. The flames had
not reached Wrights when they died
down for the night, and it is believed that
the railroad and other property there is
I in no more danger.
KESWICK, Oct. B.â For four days for
-1 est fires have been running over the Clear
Creek region west of Shasta, near the
head of Little Salt Creek. They were
starting to burn westward when the wind
changed and drove the fire back toward
the pipeline and flumes of the Princess
Hydraulic Mining company. Property
representing thousands of dollars In value
\ was In danger of destruction. For two
days and nights a large crew of fire-fight
i ers strove to save the valuable property.
It was hot work, but the efforts were sue
: cessful and further danger averted. The
i fire Is well under control now.
COVELO, Oct. B.â Destructive forest
fires are raging from Covelo to Layton
viii,, and Cahto to Westport, sweeping
everything Inflammable In their path.
The damage by the fire to property
owners will exceed $35,000. The losses es
timated include construction material ot
the Pacific telephone and Telegraph
Company, Western Union Telegraph
Company Vassar Lumber Company and
numerous tie and lumber camps. Some
500 fire fighters have maintained a vig
ilant watch for the past twenty-four
hours Many persons were prostrated by
the heat The Odd Fellows' Cemetery at
Laytonville was badly damaged. The flro
Is not yet under control, and Is estimated
to cover about sixty miles.
SANTA CRUZ, Oct. Dense smoke
still obscures the mountains on account
of forest fires. Ashes and cinders fell to
day near this city. The fires near Boulder
Creek are under control and no further
danger Is feared. The fire In the neigh
borhood of Pescadero Creek and In the
direction of Alma apparently continues to
rape fiercely, judging from the immense
volume of smoke arising In that direc
tion The fire is being kept in the under
brush on a territory covering four or five
miles If it is kept there no fear Is felt
for the safety of the tall redwood trees
and cordwood west of the fire. If the
flames should manage to break through
the territory they are now confined to
they would "have plenty of dry material
to feed on and cause much loss. An army
of men is now fighting vlgorusly trying to
prevent Its spread. No reports of heavy
Individual losses have as yet reached this
city The fire was caused either by care
less' hunters or through men clearing
brush from land permit ing it to get be
-5 SACRAMENTO, Oct. B.â Last night a
timber and brush fire In the mountains
near Cisco threatened the destruction of
more snowsheds, but the railroad com
pany sent out fire trains and protected
the "sheds. Several trains were delayed by

Sacramento Wins.
SACRAMENTO, Oct. 8. -The ball game
between the Sacramento Athletic- Club
and the Oakland Acme Athletic Club
teams played here to-day was a tame
affair and resulted in a victory for the
local team by a score of 20 to 4. Flege
was touched for seventeen hits, two of
which were home runs. Batteriesâ W.
Newbert und Conway; Fiege and Peralta,
few words to the colored folk. The dimly
lighted little chapel, with its humble ap
pointments, was in strange contrast to
the magnificence of the scene with the
services earlier in the day.
The President said: "My friends, It
gives me very great pleasure to meet
with you on this memorable day. The
noblest sentiments of the human heart
after love of God is love of country, and
that includes love of home, the corner
stone of strength and safety. Your race
has demonstrated its patriotism by its
sacrificesâ lts love of the flag by dying
for It. That is the greatest test of fidel
ity and loyalty. The nation has appre
ciated the valor and patriotism of the
black men of the United States. They not
only fought In Cuba, but in the Philip
pines, and they are carrying the flag as
the symbol of liberty and hope to an op
pressed people?'
After breakfast, which the President
ate with Mrs. McKinley and Miss Bar
ber, Mr. McKinley received Informal calls
from members of the Presidential party.
When the start shortly before noon was
about to be made for the home of La
fayette McWllllams, where President and
Mrs. McKinley and Miss Barber had ar
ranged to dine, Mrs. McKinley upset the
plans of the arrangement committee. The
committee had planned that Charles
Truax. B. J. Rosenthal and E. C. Keith
should ride with the city's distinguished
guests.
At the last moment Mrs. McKinley an
nounced her own desire to have the com
pany of her husband during the ride. The
disappointed trio of committeemen ac
cepted this with good grace and climbed
into a carriage by themselves.
Whenever the President appeared in
his carriage on the street he was fol
lowed by a throng of people in all sorts
of vehicles from the bicycle to the auto
mobile. So persistent and withal so
pretty was little Harry Allen Storm, who,
mounted on his wheel, in his Lord
Fauntleroy toggery, folowed by the Presi
dent, that upon his arrival at the hotel
from the home of Captain McWllllams,
the nation's chief sent for the boy, shook
the lad's hand and covered him with
confusion by his kindly words of greet
ing.
Over 6000 persons were present at the
Auditorium to-night, attending the union
religious service. Hundreds were turned
away. The appearance of President Mc-
Kinley was the signal for wild applause.
The entire audience arose and handker
chiefs waved the Chautauqua salute.
The President stood in his box and bowed
to the audience repeatedly. Mrs. Mc-
Kinley did not attend the services.

cus Triumphal March" was rendered by

BRYAN MUST HAVE
ABSOLUTE REST
Free Silver Champion Is
Seriously 111.
Special Dispatch to x ThÂ« Call.
CHICAGO, Oct. 8.-A special to the
Chronicle from Webster, lowa, says: Will
lam J. Bryan is a very sick man to-day.
Friends who have spent Sunday with him
at the farm of Fred F. White, Democratic
candidate for Governor of lowa, three
miles from Webster, are afraid that Mr.
Bryan may have to temporarily abandon
his campaign work, not only In lowa, but
in Kentucky and Ohio.
When Mr. Bryan awoke this morning
he complained about his throat and lungs,
but said that he had suffered from a simi
lar ailment once before, and that it had
yielded to treatment.
Great crowds of farmers from all over
Keokuk County called at the White farm
to see Mr. Bryan during the morning and
afternoon, and although he tried hard to
remain outdoors his physical weakness
mastered his courage and he had to retire
several times to his room. A local phy
sician who was called in to-night to treat
the Invalid recommended a long term of
absolute rest and quiet.
It is understood to-night that steps will
be taken by interested friends to cancel
Mr. Bryan engagements, which Include
two and three speeches daily and a tour
covering the width of lowa, ending at
Sioux City Saturday. 4
WALTER WELLMAN
REACHES NEW YORK
Arctic Explorer Arrives on Steam
ship TJmbria and Goes to
Washington.
NEW YORK. Oct. Walter Wellman.
the Arctic explorer who recently returned
from a Journey of exploration on
Franz Josef Land, arrived in this city
on the steamship Umbria. He was ac
companied from England by his wife.
This afternoon Mr. and Mrs. Wellman
took a train for their home in Washing
ton. The severe injuries the explorer re
ceived from a fall In an ice crevasse still
kept him upon crutches and he had to be
assisted from the ship by his wife and a
friend.
Mr. Wellman left Tromsoe, Norway in
June of last year for the purpose of 'ex
ploring Franz Josef Land. His progress
was arrested by the accident in which
his leg was Injured. When asked to-day
about the accident he said: "When once
sledge dogs get stuck with their load In
rough Ice they are more obstinate than
mules and you have got to help them out
yourself. On March 20, my dogs got stuck
In rough Ice and I got out of the sled
took off their harness and put it around
my chest and shoulders, Intending to do
my own teaming until the dogs became
more tractable. As I was walking alone
head down and hauling my sled, I put mv
foot into a crack in the Ice eighteen
inches deep and so covered with snow as
to be hidden. My right leg went down
the crevasse, scraping the shinbone and
otherwise Injuring the limb. a
"For eight consecutive days I walked
across the ice thus injured and then fell
exhausted. My companions helped me un
put me on a sledge and dragged me to
the nearest retreat, the headquarters
house at Cape Tegelhoff, where I remain
ed in bed, crippled from April 9 to July 4
In August I went to London for surgical
treatment. I live in the hope that I am
not permanently crippled."

Son of the Grand Vizier Shot by an
Albanian.
CONSTANTINOPLE. Oct. 8.-Jarid Bey
son of Helil Rifat ,Pasha, the Grand
Vizier, was assassinated on the Galata
bridge to-day by an Albanian, who fired
four shots from a revolver. The mur
derer was arrested.
Munir Pasha, the Sultan's grand master
of ceremonies, is dead. He wll be suc
ceeded by Ibrahim Raslsch Bey, the In
troducer of Embassadors.

T'LRICHVILLE. Ohio, Oct. B.â James
Brady, a bridge-jumper of Pittsburg, was

scious. Brady is paralyzed and will die.
