BUSINESS GETTING AHEAD OF CASH
SOME weeks ago it was seen that the remarkable
activity in trade was drawing large sums from
the banks into general commercial circulation,
but it was thought that the condition was merely tem
porary. It is now seen, however, that it is becoming
more pronounced and that there is a growing scarcity
of the circulating medium. The stringency has been
most marked in England, and from time to time the
Bank of England has advanced its rate of discount in
order to prevent the outflow of gold to foreign coun
tries and especially America, ami last week two ad
vances were made, raising the rate from 3 / 2 to 5 per
cent, which is very high. The stringency is also
severe in Germany, as shown by the action of the
Bank of Germany in also raising its rate to 6 per
cent. These advances will probably check the exports
of gold to the United States, especially as the Bank of
France has also lost gold heavily of late and is taking
similar measures to keep the yellow coin at home.
Of course these conditions affect New York, where
money is now high, being wanted in large amounts
for speculation and legitimate trade. There is no
real stringency in New York as in the three other
centers, however, in spite of the high rates of interest,
some borrowers being compelled to pay to(Â§ 15 per
cent, while 8(o!io per cent were the easiest rates ob
tainable last week. Many operators considered these
high rates altogether unnecessary and the result of
manipulation, but it is admitted that 6 per cent will
be the minimum rate for some weeks to come, as New
York is being heavily drawn upon for funds to move
the Western crops. It is believed, however, that in
the early winter months currency will return to New
York a* it usually does when the crops begin to
move, when the money market will naturally ease off.
This, at least, is the expectation of Wall street, which
looks for a quiet stock market until that time arrives.
There is no stringency sufficiently severe, however,
to cause any apprehension. The Transvaal situation
is an important factor in the case, as it is broadly
asserted in some quarters that England needs the gold
mines there to recuperate her diminished supply of
gold, though English authorities pooh-pooh the bare
idlea of such a necessity.
The fact remains, however, that in this country the
needs of a phenomenally active commerce , both
domestic and foreign, arc exceeding the immediate
supply of currency, and Congress will probably be
called upon to reinforce it in some manner. When
business is as rushing as it has been for a year or two.
large amounts of money are required to carry it on,
hence as far as the money market is concerned a
moderate falling off in the volume of trade would not
be unwelcome at the moment. But there arc few signs
of any 'decrease at present. True, the distributive
trade is reported quieter in some sections, and some
States, notably California, Indiana, New Hampshire,
New Jersey and several of the Territories, show
slightly increased liabilities in failures, though, the
country at large shows less failures than at this time
last year, the suspensions being remarkably small, as
repeatedly remarked in this column. The great
staples, however, continue as active as ever, and the
bank clearances at New York, despite the interruptions
caused by the Dewey reception and the yacht race,
are larger than ever before. Thus, in spite of a- tight
money market at the country's financial center, the
business of the nation continues heavier than ever
before known. And the gratifying feature of it is
that it is apparently a genuine business, a legitimate
demand for all sorts of goods. It is a curious condi
tion, to say the least. Generally, when money ad
vances and becomes somewhat scarce trade falls off.
For the rest there is nothing new. Prices for all
commodities continue firm at the recent sharp ad
vances, and mills and factories are turning down
orders all over the country. , The demand for pro->
visions is so sharp in the West that the great packing
houses there are from a week to a fortnight behind
in their orders; and it has been often observed that
when provisions arc active business is brisk all over
the land.
The Mechanics' Institute of this city jis making a
record as a splendid school for librarians. Last week
two of its attaches were given positions in other li
braries. One went to take charge of the Mercantile
and the other of the public library at Alameda.
Even the worm will turn. The Citizens' Republican
party, stung by treacherous ingratitude, has' decided
trwit if Dan Burns wants any more touts he will have
to look to his stables.
THE TRAFFIC ASSOCIATION.
tZ__JOR some time past the merchants of St. Louis,
Chicago and other important points of the
Mississippi Valley have been engaged in an
effort to induce the Interstate Commerce Commission
to so change the existing freight differentials as to
' give to those cities material advantages over the cities
of the Atlantic and the Pacific .coasts. The movement
I urged under the lead of the St. Louis Association has
I now become a menace to the coast jobbers, and it is
; clear that unless something be done to counteract it
j the results may be seriously hurtful to our interests,
I not only in the city but throughout the State.
Under such circumstances it is gratifying to note
that the merchants of San Francisco have organized
to conserve the trade interests of California and the
coast. The Traffic Association which has just been
formed has been brought together and established
mainly for this contest. In fact Charles Holbrook
on taking the chair at the first meeting of the pro
moters of the movement said:
"We are called for the purpose of organizing a
traffic association to combat the proposition of the St.
; Louis Association, which has asked for lower differen
tials to intermediate coast points, and also for graded
rates from the Atlantic Coast to St. Louis and
j Chicago. Its effort is to secure a rate lower than that
enjoyed by San Francisco. If St. Louis can ship ioo
pounds to any interior point cheaper than we can
i ship a carload out here,- it is plain that the San Fran
cisco jobber will have to go out of business."
From the earnestness with which . our merchants
I have entered into the contest, it is evident the time
, when they were content to do business "indifferent to
fate" has gone' by. They are now aroused to the
I necessity of entering into an incessantly active com
| petition for trade and of being ever alert and
j vigorous. That in itself is a good sign of the new
era, and the city and thc State are to be congratulated
I on the change from the old order of things.
The San Francisco Association . will not have to
1 fight the battle alone. Our dispatches of yesterday
| announced that the large commercial houses of Bos
: ton, New York, Philadelphia and of Galveston realize
. the importance of the" issue to them and would on
their side oppose the aggressive pretensions of the St.
! Louis and Chicago boomers. It is reasonable to
assume that the merchants of the Puget Sound cities,
of Portland and of Los Angeles and San Diego will
join also in the contest, for in proportion to their
trade the issue affects them as much as San Fran
cisco.
Thequestion.it must be borne in mind, concerns not
only the jobbing centers but all the country round
j about them. San Francisco is the great market and
depot for all California products and whatever injures
the city will in the end be injurious to every interest
of the State at large. The Traffic Association, there
fore, merits the hearty co-operation of the people
generally in its contest for the commercial interests
of the coast, since it has for its object the welfare of
; all.
The wide distribution of appealing circulars
throughout . the country is authority for the belief
that Major General Miles wants to be President of the
United States. There are probably several other
things that the general wants and can't get.
The life of Poundmaster Slinkey of Sau'salito was
saved the other day through the sagacity of a yellow
dog. In common gratitude he should give up his call
ing as executioner of canines.
THE PROBLEM OF THE SOUTH
IN a recent issue of the Atlanta Constitution two col
ums were given to a description of a reception and
a banquet tendered to an honored guest on his
return from Europe: The hall. was filled by a large
gathering, including many of the most distinguished
men and women of the city. The address of wel
come was delivered by Governor Candler, and
speeches were made by the State Commissioner of
Education and other officials. Now the man to whom
all the honor was done is a negro â Booker T. Wash
ington.
â¢ When Southern officials, dependent for office upon
the votes of white men. can take prominent part in
a reception given to a negro, it is clear the worst part
of the race problem in the South is over. There is
no longer an unreasoning antagonism to the black
man, no longer an invincible prejudice against his
work and his worth. Of course the fact that Wash
ington has carefully abstained from politics and has
advised other colored men to do likewise has much
to do with the favor shown by the politicians of the
South, but it would be unjust to attribute all to that
fact. The president of the Tuskegee Institute has
done and is doing a great work not only- for his race
but for his section, and the better class of Southern
whites have not only the intelligence to recognize it
but the honesty to admit it and the justice to com
mend it.
The warmth of the welcome given to the guest of
the evening was equal to any that could be given to
the most eminent educator of the white race. The
Governor said: "I am hereto-day to say I am glad
;to welcome him back to this country ,and to the
. South. We of the South need more such men.
Especially do we need them in Georgia." Colonel
Glenn, the State School Commissioner, said: "I look
upon this great man as the peer of any man of his
day and time. I regard him as being in front of one
of the greatest movements of decades â a movement
i that will produce like good to both races."
Professor Washington began his address by saying:
"The presence of the Governor of the State 'of
. Georgia, the State Commissioner of Education, as
well as other distinguished citizens of the white race,
I who join this large and representative negro audience
on this occasion, is added proof that the noblest mem
bers of the Anglo-Saxon race in the South have a
. deep and abiding interest in' the -progress of the
negro." He then went oh to discuss the race prob
lem, and among other sanguine and encouraging
things said:
"So long as the negro is permitted unmolested 10
secure education, property,' employment and is given
| the protection of the law,' I shall have great faith in
our being able to work out our own destiny. Over
and over again, while in : Europe, I had constantly
impressed upon me the advantage which the negro
has in the South in the opportunity to enter success
! I'll business as compared with poor people' in Europe.
If you ask me for the source and foundation for my
' encouragement over the 'prospects of the race, I
would point you to the negro who is engaged in busi
ness in the South. In all parts of the South 'I have
met the negro carpenter, j truck gardener, the con
tractor, the butcher, the merchant: they speak hope
fully and encouragingly. Everywhere they tell me
j that in business in the South there is practically no
; color line. Two races are to -live in this country to
j gether, and he is an enemy to both who tries to array
I one race against the other." ...-,.-. . . ,
An incident of this kind is more illuminating to
the race problem in the South than all the specula
tions of theorists, or policies of politicians put to
THE SAIN FRANCISCO MONDAY, OCTOBER 9, 1899.
gether. It shows that among the better and the
dominant elements of both races there is a sincere
determination to do justice to one another and to
establish a sure foundation for the welfare of their
States. Booker Washington is educating more than
the pupils who resort to his. institute. The whole
nation is learning something from him, and his
influence to-day is as helpful as that of any man of
which the white, race can boast. 'â¢'.
THE TRANSVAAL SITUATION.
NOTWITHSTANDING the alarming reports of
imminent danger of war in the Transvaal which
come from South Africa, the declarations of
British statesmen on the subject continue reassuring.
It is true that troops and munitions of war are being
rapidly forwarded to the Transvaal frontier, but the
powers on both sides which make for peace are
strong, and unless something sudden and unexpected,
like the sinking of the Maine, should occur to pre
cipitate a conflict, the crisis may yet be passed in
safety.
There is a very strong peace party in Great Britain
and it has been actively at work on a campaign of
education during the last two or three weeks. One
of the most eminent among them, Mr. John Mbrley,
who represents a Scotch constituency in Parliament,
recently addressed his constituents on the issue and
pointed out clearly the folly of the course which the
jingoes are pursuing. Quoting the saying of Dean
Swift, "I have never heard that ten men armed to. the
teeth are not a match for one man in his shirt," he
assumed that in the conflict the British would be
successful, but argued that such success would be
unprofitable at present and dangerous in the future.
Warming to his subject he said: "The British and
the Dutch have got to live together in South Africa.
Fuse them. Do not say to one race, 'You are to be at
the top, and the other shall be at the bottom.' No.
Let there be fusion, not paramountcy. Are you going
to fight them for paramountcy when you know, apart
from its guilt, it can only lead to new burdens and
new responsibilities, and new difficulties? * * *
A war of that kind will not be a war with honor; it
will be a war with deep dishonor, and what a mock
ery will such a war. make of all the professions that
have been made, emphatically and in capital letters,
within- the last few months especially, upon behalf of
peace! What a faree â what a hypocritical farce to
send your important representatives to The Hague
to try whether something cannot be done to introduce
better principles into the relations between states!
What a farce, what an example for this country,
which has hitherto vaunted and boasted â and justly
boasted that it is in the front of great moral, pacific
and progressive causes! What an example for us to
set to the armed camps and the scheming. Chancel
leries of Continental Europe! What a shadow cast
upon the reign of the Queen! Yes, empire they say â
empire. Yes. but we do not want a pirate empire."
In addition to the moral aspects of the controversy
there are material considerations which have for
some time past been strongly affecting the British
mind. It has been estimated that if peace be con
tinued the gold output of the Transvaal mines will in
the coming year amount to about $100,000,000. There
is already felt something like a gold stringency in
Europe, and with the venture of providing a gold
standard of currency for India the British financiers
are not inclined to lightly shut off the bullion they
now have a right to look for from the land of the
Boers.
In addition to the valuable gold export the Trans
vaal has an annual import trade amounting to more
than $100,000,000, and the British have had the lion's
share of it. A war would mean a loss, therefore, to
manufacturers and merchants as well as to gold miners.
All of these items are being taken into account by the
British people and by British statesmen. Joseph
Chamberlain has been posing as an imperialist of the
military type, but by birth and training he is a trades
man. He knows the value of industry and com
merce. It is not likely that he will venture to run
counter to all the interests with which he has been
so long associated. Despite the gathering of the
forces on both sides, the promise of peace is there
lore good. Great Britain would of course like to
dominate the Transvaal, but she is hardly willing to
pay the price.
REVISION OF THE WAR TAX.
LATE reports from Washington confirm those
sent out some time ago to the effect that in
** order to provide a revenue sufficient to meet
the expenses of the Government, Congress will be
urged by the administration to undertake a radical
revision of the war revenue law. It is stated by the
Philadelphia Ledger that among the probabilities of
the revision are that the rigor of the law will be
increased,' defects which have resulted in a serious loss
of revenue will be corrected and new subjects for
internal revenue taxation will be ingeniously sought.
Such a revision as will cure in the existing law the
defects which have resulted in a loss of revenue will
be cordially approved by the people, but they; will
expect something more. Under the law as it stands
there has been practiced wholesale tax shirking by
some of the large corporations of the country, the
most notorious offenders being the express com
panies. It will be expected of Congress that evils of
that kind be remedied as well as those which diminish
the revenues of the Government.
The Call recently obtained from Senator Perkins
and the members of the House of Representatives
from California statements plainly declaring their
purpose to assist in putting an end to the outrageous
extortions which the tax shirkers have so long prac
ticed. It can hardly be doubted that a majority of
the House will stand with them on the issue, for the
wrong done by the express companies has not been
practiced in this State only. From every part of the
Union complaints have been heard and in several
States besides California the people have in many
cases appealed to the courts for redress.
One of the extraordinary features of the tax shirk
ing is that while the courts have uniformly declared
the plain intent of the law to be that the express com
panies shall pay the tax, the corporations ; have gone
on with the shirking as if the decisions of the courts
were worthless. Every day of delay adds' an enor
mous sum to the amount taken from the people by
the companies,- and yet for some reason the courts
seem willing to grant them delay. - . 'â¢ "â 
It will be remembered that when 1 the corporations
wished a prompt decision on the income tax law the
courts provided a way by which- final judgment was
given before the first installment of the tax was due.
Since a similar promptness cannot be obtained in the
present instance to protect the public from- the
rapacity of the corporations. Congressional action be
comes: imperative, and it will be with no little satis
faction the people will note the undertaking of the
revision of the law. â¢â â  .-â >â¢".â¢ * '"â¢..*-â¢ â "â â â¢
And they still continue to send out reports' to the
effect that the Prince. of Wales is giddy.'- As if any
thing else was to be expected of a man in such an
exalted position.

COMMENDS ITSELF TO ALL.

J. V. Tuttle of Omaha arrived here
yesterday to assume the position of chief
clerk under General Agent W. D. San
born of the Burlington Route.
Captain A. D. Miller of Seattle. W. M.
Wyatt of Fresno and Thomas Fennell of
Portland. Or., are at the Grand from Cape
Nome, where they have claims on Anvil
Creek. They brought with them some
fine nuggets that assay $19 to the ounce.
Five thousand people at Anvil City are
waiting to get out before winter sets in.
Coal and lumber are scarce and there will
be a shortage of provisions if some do not
succeed in petting away. Wages range
from $7 to $20 a day, and there will be
plenty, of work next winter. They con
sider Nome and Oolovin Bay a better
country than Dawson, and will go back
in the spring.
R. S. Forbes, representing a London
electrical concern, arrived from Peru yes
terday and is at the lack. He has just
closed contracts for the electric lighting
of Lima, and will return shortly to ar
range for some projected electric roads.
He says that the country is in splendid
commercial and financial condition. A
steam road Is contemplated from Arroya
to the silver mines at Cerro de Pasco.
The newly elected President. Edouardo
Romano, has succeeded Don Nicolas Ple
rola. who four years ago, at the head of
a revolutionary party, drove President
races out of power and served his con
stitutional term with great credit. p
NEWS OF FOREIGN NAVIES.
The Japanese battleship Shik'shim.i was
recently docked at the Portsmouth dock
yard and is now progressing with a series
of trials, which will take about two
months.
The German cruiser Victoria Lu'.se of
5650 tons, built at Bremen, has passed
through her forced draught trial, develop
ing a speed of 19.1 knots. The contract
call< d for 20 knots.
The British battleship London, built at
the -Portsmouth dock yard, had the keel
laid December 7, IS9S, and was launched
September 21. taking less than nine
months to build and half completing a
15,000-ton ship.
Through the Introduction of modern
guns aboard ships In the German navy
there has been effected a considerable re
duction in the; personnel. The Branden
burg's complement of 575 tun been cut
down to 552; Sachsen from 410 to 375 and
Slegfreid from 255 to 225. ?.;- .
The British cruiser Powerful has left
the China station and is due at Ports
mouth, October 23. Her place . will be
taken by the sister ship Terrible. Both
vessels will pass through the Suez Canal
and their draught, which in seagoing con
dition is 27 feet, will have to be reduced
to 25 feet 7 inches. This will be effected by
taking out all the coal except the neces
sary quantity to steam through the canal.
The annual depreciation of modern war
vessels has been figured by Japanese navy
officials to range as follows on the first
cost: Armored ships, 3.9 per cent; un
armored vessels. 5.4 per cent, and 6.5 per
cent on torpedo craft. According to this
showing an armored ship completed dur
ing the present year would in ten years
hence only represent a value of 61 per cent
of her first cost, an unarmored vessel 46
per cent and a torpedo craft would be
worth only 35 per cent of the original cost.
As, however, it is reasonable --to assume
that the vessels had been kept in good I
repairs during this period at an average j
expenditure of say 2 per cent per annum; i
the alleged depression should be reduced j
correspondingly. As a matter of fact the j
hulls of the two first types are practically j
indestructible if properly cared for; the j j
motive power may also be preserved for i
a great number of years with proper j
treatment, and the only serious and un
preventable depreciation of the' vessels
enumerated is due to. the fact that they
rapidly become obsolete, and â¢ there are
scores of these in the several navies.; ...
A third-class sheathed cruiser, named
Infernet, was launched at Bordeaux Sep
tember 7. She is 2452 tons displacement,
311 feet 8 inches In length. 39 feet 4 inches
breadth and 15 feet 6 inches draught. The
engines, of 8500 horsepower, are to give a
speed of 20*4 knots. Her battery embraces
two six-Inch, four three and a half-Inch,
eight six-pounders and ten one and a half
pounders. The launching, took place with
the engines and boilers' in the ship, and
she] will be rapidly completed. 'Ââ¢''.'â ; ,
The Canopus, British battleship of 12.950
tons, recently completed, passed through
her eight-hour full-power trial on Sep
tember 12. With 289 pounds of steam the
revolutions averaged 10S5, developing 13,763
horsepower and giving a speed by log of
15.5 knots.' The coal consumption was only
1.72 pounds per horsepower per hour. The
designed horsepower was 13,500, which
was exceeded by 263. but the intended
speed of 18.75 knots, fell short by one
fourth of a knot, if the log recorded accu
rately. The ship was down to a draught
of twenty-six feet on an even keel, which
exceeds the draught legend by seven
inches, or about 350 tons extra displace-]
ment.
Great difficulty was experienced in
building the Oriental drydock at Shang
hai. The soil was muddy sand, without
cohesion, and to secure a solid foundation
3500 twelve-inch piles of twenty-four foot
length were driven over the area of the
bottom. These piles were capped longi
tudinally, upon which again transverse
stringers were laid, all of which timber
work was bedded in seven feet of con
crete and floored with three-inch plank.
The keel-blocks are spaced with four-foot
centers, with six piles under each block,
and when recently a vessel of war weigh
ing 7000 tons was docked there was no
sign of sagging or other infirmities of the
structure.
A sheathed cruiser of 6375 tons is to be
built at the new Russian Government
yard at Windau. The vessel is to be 412
feet in length, 53 feet 6 inches beam and
20 feet 8 inches draught. The engines,
with twelve Normand boilers, will devel
op 20.000 horsepower to give a speed of
23 knots. lucre will be three funnels and
two military masts and the armament
will be composed of four S-lnch and two
turrets, twelve 6-inch Canet quick-firers
of 45 calibers, of which eight in casemates
and four on upper deck; twelve 3-inch
50-callber quick-firers in battery and eight
6-pounder Hotchkiss, besides five torpedo
tubes,' of which two are under water. The'
cruiser will have the unusual high free
board of 20 feet 6 inches.

TO CLEAN SHELLSâ J., City. The fol
lowing is given as a method of cleaning
shells: Make a lye by boiling strong
ashes; allow it to settle; pour the lye over
the shells and boil them six or seven
hours, or longer If they are large. Then
soak and wash often in fresh water.

right to locate a mining claim, and she j
may locate on the. same lode With ncr
husband and adjoining his location. A i
minor could locate a claim, but a minor !
cannot give a power of attorney. - Without ;
a power of attorney a father could not
locate for his minor child, but he could .
make a location as trustee for his child. ,

Subscribers Are Indignant Because
All Information Regarding the
Plans of the Society Are
Withheld.
There la music in the air. symphony
music at that, and some jarring notes.
Some of the most prominent members of
the Symphony Society are indignant, be
cause they have so far been unable to
obtain a satisfactory statement from the
secretary in regard to the funds and
plans of the society iir this season. They
claim that unless an official session Is
called before the fifteenth of this month
they will call a special meeting and de
mand information as to the standing of
the society. In fact, the date for the spe
cial meeting has been fixed for the
15th of this month and notices to this
effect will be forwarded to the members
unless an official meeting is called by the
directors before that date. ;
The members having regularly sub
scribed to the concerts believe themselves
entitled to inforr anon as to the work of
the society, usually the symphony sea
son commences September 15 or there
abouts, and now almost a month has
past since that date and nothing has been
done. It is asserted that the surplus fund
should amount to about 52.000, and that
this sum is sufficient to reorganize a new
series of concerts. â  â¢ -;
Dr. H. J. Stewart, president of the so
ciety, claims that, on account of the fact
that the musicians have raised their rates
from $5 to J*, it would be financial sui
cide to give a series of concerts this sea
son, and in this contention he seems to
have some â¢ supporters. - Other members
claim, however, that at least an effort
should be made to solicit subscribers and
notify the members to renew their sub
scriptions. Such an effort may turn out
to be so successful that a new series of
concerts would be a possibility. *
Secretary Harold Wheeler claims that
efforts had been made to call a meeting,
but they were in vain, while the members
state again that they have so far not
received any notice. Harry H. Campbell
claims that* he settled with the secretary
at the end of last season and that the
society was in nourishing circumstances.
It would seem, therefore, that the mcm
i bers are corroborated in their claim that
I something should be done to revive mat-
I ters. ' â¢<',-. . â¢â¢ .
Another obstacle that seems to be ad
i vanced by one side is the lack of a
! leader. To this the members present the
i names of three capable gentlemen, Oscar
i "Weil, who has led symphony concerts be
! fore and proved a successful conductor;
Max Hirschfeld. who has proved his effi
ciency on many occasions, and Henry
Holmes, for whom a petition is being cir
culated by many prominent members of
the society. Fpon being asked whether
Hiev were willing to accept the position,
each of these gentlemen declared in the
affirmative. â¢ â¢ "v.
Having sufficient funds and there being
no Jack. of a leader the members do not
see why there should be any more delay
i in the matter, and a number of them
! have therefore decided to take some ac
; tion and agitate a movement to reorgan
ize. Mr. Campbell states that a large
number of the, old subscribers have sent
in their applications for new cards and
are now impatiently waiting for informa
tion. Upon inquiry it was found that
a great many of the subscribers are anx-
I ious to have a new series of concerts, and
j while Fritz Scheel was the most efficient
i man they could have desired for this po
sition they do not see why another capa
; ble musician cannot give San Francisco a
I series of symphony concerts which It
needs and to discontinue which would be
j musical suicide.
CALL'S TRIUMPH APPRECIATED
Stockton Independent.
THe Call and New York Herald seem
to have scored quite a success by the
wireless telegraph used in connection with
the yacht race. xhe bulletins appear to
have been both frequent and accurate.
Of course, we cannot expect the com
petitors of those papers to make this ad
mission, but in the interest of truth and
science it must be recognized.
Odd Telegrams.
Excitement is often the cause of strange
telegrams as well as of strange manifes
tations. A man who had been one of the
passengers on a shipwrecked vessel was
rescued almost by a miracle. On arriving
at the place from which he could send a
telegraphic message he forwarded the
following dispatch to his brother: "I am
saved. Try to break it to my wife."â

New Pastor Proves an Earnest and
Forceful Speaker' Rev. W. H.
Ratcliff Celebrant at the
Tenth Anniversary.
The tenth anniversary of the Church or
fit. Mary the "Virgin was celebrated with
impressive services yesterday morning,
and was marked by the first appearance
In the pulpit of the new pastor, Rev. J.
Brelthwaite Bartlett.
The dedication services were opened
with a solemn procession led by the cele
brant. Rev. Father W. H. Ratellff, ac-
REV. J. BREITnWAITE BARTLETT.
companled by Arthur Post el, master nt
ceremonies; Waldo Postel, cruclner;
Charles Cook, thurifter, four torch-bear
ers and a number of acolytes.
Notwithstanding the edict of the
Archbishop of England, declaring illegal
the burning of incense and the carrying
of processional lights, both of these fea
tures formed an interesting part of the
ceremonies. The edict was the subject of
a sermon delivered by Father Ratcliff re
cently, in which he said that the Arch
bishop's prohibition of incense entirely
and of the use of candles In processions
would not be accepted in this country,
because they grounded their opinion on
an old act of Parliament, passed in
Queen Elizabeth's time. It is the opin
ion of Anglican divines in America that
the decision does not apply to American
churches, as it concerns matters of form
and not of faith. It had not been gener
ally accepted in England, and would not
be here.
After the procession J. T. Draper's mass
was sung by an augmented choir under
the leadership of Miss Homer, the or

gainst, isiamer's â¢&anctus." an old
Anglican chant, "Sursum Corda," and
the communion hymn, "Hail to the
Body," were also-intoned.
In his sermon Father Bartlett took for
his text the eleventh verse of the third
chapter of First Corinthians. "For other
foundation can 'no man lay than that is
laid, which Is Jesus Christ." Father Bart
lett proved himself an earnest, forceful
speaker and quite won the hearts of his
congregation -by his quiet buc effective
oratory. He spoke of the foundation of
the spiriitual structure, which is not ma
terial in character but is In each Individ
ual soul.
"St. Paul's great faith In Jesus Christ,"
said Father Bartlett. "prompted him to
utter the words of the text I have chosen.
Jesus Christ has laid the foundation of
his church and priests and Bishops are
merely instruments. It is not Just that
the latter should be regarded as superior
to the cnurch Itself. We often hear peo
ple say, 'I belong to Mr. So and So's
church, or I like Mr. Blank's preaching.'
My impression is that individual teach
ing jor preaching is not the standard, but
the church itself should be given the im
portance It possesses as the temple of
Jesus Christ.
"You that have been baptized should
rear a temple on that foundation of faith
that will be worthy in the sight of God.
We should feel the need of making our
selves fit to stand In his presence on the
last day."
Father Bartlett was born In Buffalo, Is
33 years of age and a graduate of the
Western Theological Seminary of Chi
cago. He resigned as rector of St. Paul,
Long Island, to accept the vacancy made
by Rev. Herbert Parrish, who is now
pastor of the Church of the Advent.
MISS LYONS WORK
AMONG THE ARABS
Miss Mary Lyons, who for eighteen
months past was one of the two workers
in the Peniel Mission at Port Said, Egypt. !
was the guest of the local mission at 49
Third street yesterday afternoon. Miss
Lyons is a Jewess, her grandfather hay- I
ing been chief rabbi of Jerusalem. They '
were both converted to the Christian re- >
ligion at Mount Zion, where the daughter
was born. i
Miss Lyons told of her conversion by
Miss Vansant, one of the Port Said work
ers, who visited Jerusalem during a se- I
vere illness, from which she afterward
died.
"I became interested in the good work,"
said Miss Lyons, "through the kindly
character of Miss Vansant. Her lovable
ways and her implicit faith in the Lord
so impressed me that I joined her at Port
Said and entered the work, to which I am
anxious to return.
"There is much to do in Egypt, especi
ally among the little girl children whom
the Moslems do not regard very highly.
We have established a school which be
gan with twenty pupils, but now numbers
thirty-five little ones, who are learning
the word of God. We go among the poor
people in their wretched mud huts and
experience no difficulty in persuading pa
rents to allow their little daughters to at
tend the school. We have become very
much encouraged in the saving of these
little souls. They are eager to be taught
the lessons in the Bible and several are
able to repeat from memory a number of
verses In the holy book."
To-morrow afternoon there will be an
other meeting and Miss Lyons will wear
the Arab costume which she uses when
working among the natives.
NO DESIRE TO ECONOMIZE.
School Directors Destroy Desks That
Could Have Been Repaired.
The latest School Board scandal prem
ises to give the Directors who are at pres
ent undergoing Grand Jury investigation
a great deal of trouble before the end is
reached. The deal In which the Supplies
Committee la now involved tends to show
that Directors Conlon and Gedge have lit
tle regard for the welfare of the School
Department in the expenditure of the
funds. Their extravagance became so no
torious seme months ago that the board
shut down on them and limited them to
an outlay of $2500 a month. Instead of
obeying instructions they ran up in three
months' time bills amounting to $30,000.
The Weber & Co. claim for $10,000 for
desks was the subject of special consid
eration Friday night by the Finance Com
mittee. Conlon told the committee before
it met that he had only contracted a bill
for $3500, and In tho face of that positive
statement the Arm produced jConlon'a re
quisitions for the amount claimed. The
bill Is for (jesks, and in this connection it
is interesting, according to Director Head
that more tiian 500 desks were destroyed
a short time ago by tho supply depart
ment, which could have been repaired at
little cost had there been the least desire
on the part of Conlon and Gedge to be
economical.
The dismissal of Grant Cole, assistant
storekeeper, followed closely upon the dis
covery of the last scandal and tho an
nouncement of the smashing of the second
band desks. Although Cole's father Is a
member of the board he could not save
hi.* son's official head. Cole says that his
dismissal was in the line of economy, but
he does not like the reference Director
Head made to the unnecessary destruc
tion of desks Just before the ax fell. The
members of the board Insist that Cole's
dismissal had nothing to do with the im
pending scandal.
The Finance Committee has decided not
to allow Weber & Co.'s claim and the firm
will have to fight it out with Conlon and
Gedge, who ordered the goods.

GRACE CHURCH
CELEBRATES ITS
GOLDEN JUBILEE
Historical Sermon by
Dr. R. C. Foute.
- â¦â '
NOTED DIVINES PRESENT
m
A BEATJTTPTJL AND ELABORATE
MUSICAL PROGRAMME.
Â«
The Offertory Is Set Apart as the
Beginning of the Endowment
Fund. of the
Parish.
Grace Episcopal Church celebrated Its
golden anniversary yesterday with im
pressive and beautiful services. The
imposing structure of brick and stone at
the corner of California and Stockton
streets has become, in the course of
years, one of the landmarks of the city.
Founded in the historic year of 1849, it
has grown from modest beginnings and a
small congregation to its present pros
perity and strength.
Its seating capacity was tested to the
utmost yesterday morning. The congrega
tion of Grace Church has Its full share of
the social leaders and conservative
wealthy families of this city, and
the scene presented at the por
tals of the church after the ser
vices was one that will not be soon
forgotten by those who witnessed it.
Many Episcopal clergymen were present,
among them being Bishop Nichols, Arch-
Deacon Emery, Rev. M. D. Wilson sec
retary of the convention; Rev. Professor
Lincoln of the Church Divinity School;
Rev. Dr. Shaw of St. Luke's and the Rev.
Dr. McClure of Oakland.
The sermon of the Rev. R. C. Foute was
replete with Interest to the congregation,
and was given the closest attention. It
was an historical review of the church's
history, Ailed with many statistics of Its
growth. Dr. Foute closed his address
with a strong and hopeful view of the
future of the church, which has accom
plished so much In the past.
The fiftieth anniversary of the church
was especially marked by the purposes to
which the offertory taken yesterday are
to be devoted. The amount is to be set
apart, and is the beginning of the endow
ment fund of the parish. The church
workers expect to increase this fund
rapidly.
The music, under the supervision of
REV. R. C. FOUTE.
William H. Holt, the organist and di
rector, was rich in spiritual Inspiration
and impressive in its beauty. The volume
of sound in the "Hallelujah" chorus, the
opening anthem, and in the "Te Deum"
and "Jubilate," from the sixteen male
voices of the choir, swelled and vibrated
In sweetest harmony throughout tho lofty
edifice. The offertory rendered was
"Praise Jehovah," by Schubert.
In the evening the regular Sunday
services wore rendered.
THE SABBATH DAY.
Sermon by the Rev. F. B. Cherington
of Plymouth Church.
The Rev. F. B. Cherington preached last
evening at the Plymouth Congregational
Church on the text "Remember the Sab
bath Day to Keep It Holy." In part he
said:
One. of the most priceless gifts of God to men
Is the Sabbath. One day In seven set apart
by the holiest sanction to rest. Left to him
self man never would have devised It. His
own greed and that of his employers would
have prevented it. Even If once given as an
Institution it could not have been preserved
except by the most solmenly binding religious
guards and injunctions. The physical man
needs the regularly recurring rest. The in
tellectual nature needs the opportunity it
affords. The spiritual nature requires the
sacred privileges It brings. Every Interest of
the Individual Is fostered by it, the home life
is strongly strengthened by It, the social and
civil life is broadened and ennobled by it. In
all Its features and all its results It bears
evidence of being a direct gift of an all-loving
and all- wise Heavenly Father to his children.
Jfafrzhu&lnv
Is Invaluable to nurslnpr mothers, feeble chil
dren, the aged and Infirm. Its merit Is as
sured, being prepared by the Anheuser-Busch
Brewing Ass'n.
Gunning for His "Wife.
James Glffen, alias Grlbben. while under
the Influence of liquor, entered the Thalia,
Market and Turk streets, last night and
threatened to shoot his wife, who was
drinking there. He was arrested by Offi
cers Coleman and McGee, who found a
revolver on his person. He was taken
to the City Prison and booked for carry
ing concealed weapons.
TULARE CAPITALIST
DIES IN THIS CITY
SOLOMON SWEET, a prominent busi
ness man of Tulare County, was
was summoned by death on Saturday
evening at his residence in this city.
He was born in New Worms, Ba
varia, seventy-two years ago. At eighteen
he reached New Tork. Several years later
he set out for California, arriving here
in 1851. He mined in Marposa and a few
years later opened a general merchandise
store at Aqua Frio. In 1857 he removed
to Visalia and there laid the foundation
of the S. Sweet Company, one of the most
successful houses of Tulare County.
In connection with Julius Jacob and
Charles Hyde, Sweet built and was for
many years a director in the Goshen and
Visalia Railroad. He also identified him
self with tho resources of the San Joa-
quin, becoming one of the largest grain
land owners In the valley. He was also
president of the California Fruit and Land
Company.
Mr. Sweet leaves a widow and several
grown up children. The funeral will be
held under the auspices of the Masons,
of which organization he was a prominent
member for many years.
"SILVER" LANE,
THE UNCROWNED
"KING OF NOME"
Brings Back Another
Fortune.
* â '
ARRIVES ON THE TOWNSEND
â¦
LATJRADA AGAIN NARROWLY
ESCAPES DESTRUCTION.
She Left Seattle Overloaded and Had
Twice to Put in and Discharge
Cargo on Her Way to the
Golden Cape.
The steamer Townsend returned yester
day morning from Cape Nome via Dutch
Harbor with news of the steamer I
Laurada's narrow escape from destruc
tion and with C. D. Lane, the millionaire j
mine owner, aboard. -
The Utlca mine has paid Mr. Lane many
a good dividend, but he expects Cape !
Nome to pay him thousands of dollars 'â 
for every cent he has ever taken out of
THE UNFORTUNATE STEAMER LAURADA.
the famous quartz mine. As a small
sample of what he will get out of Nome.
Lane brought with him on the Townsend
five sacks containing about 600 pounds of
gold dust and nuggets which he values at
$150,000. Not a bad stake for a first sea
son's venture.
C. D. Lane was one of the first rich men
to reach Cape Nome and he went pre
pared to buy and also to stay. He char
tered a schooner on Puget Sound, loaded
her with lumber and coal and sent her to
Nome. He purchased the Townsend here
and built a small launch called the G<>ss
lin. The Town-end he loaded with sup
plies and the Gosslin he sent north on a
steam schooner.
Everything reached Cape Nome in safe
ty and < * D. Lane's camp near Anvil City
is the finest and best equipped in the dis
trict. His brother-in-law had been on
the ground months before Lane's advent,
and he had staked out a number of rich
claims, Mr. Lane followed suit, and
whenever he saw a valuable location he
secured 11 11 the owner would part with
it at a reasonable figure. Thus, by pur
chase, he secured many valuable claims,
and his grub-staked men secured others.
Lane will be "King of Cape Nome" next
season, as his mines are some of the best
in the district.
"When Lane goes to Cape Nome next
year it will not be in the Townsend. He
will cither purchase or build a steamer
capable of carrying 2000 tons and in it
he will travel to the new El Dorado. He
says the richness of Nome has not yet
been fully realized. Gold is everywhere
and as plentiful in the sands of the ocean
as in the creek beds. It will be a hard
winter for those who remain this year,
as the shelter Is very bad and fuel scarce.
While the Townsend was in Dutch Har
bor the old filibustering steamer Laurada
put in there in distress. She has been
having a very hard time of it, and once
struck on the rocks and came very near
to being wrecked. The crew of the Town
send say the Laurada was overloaded
when she left Puget Sound. Soon aftei
passing out through the straits of Juan
de Fuca her troubles began. Her cap
tain took the inland passage, but that did
not save the overladen vessel. In the
heavy weather she became unwieldy and
finally the captain had to put into a port
of call and discharge some of his cargo.
The stuff was put out on the beach and
a member of the crew left to keep watch
over it.
After discharging the Laurada put to
sea again, but the heavy weather contin
ued, and the captain, finding that she was
still overloaded, put Into Matlaketla.
where he discharged a lot, more of his
cargo. After that the vessel seemed to
be all right, but before she reached
Dutch Harbor she went on a ledge â¢of
rock, and for a time It looked as though
she was doomed. She, however, slid off,
and made the harbor without any more
mishaps. The captain was looking for the
steamer Cleveland, now in the neighbor
hood of Cape Nome and St. Michael, and
if he could charter her he would send her
back after the goods put ashore on the
way up. The season is now about over,
however, and the people on the Town
send do not believe the Cleveland could
get down to Matlaketla and back again
before the Ice closed In.
The old Laurada has been very unfor
tunate. A few years ago she was nearly
wrecked oft St. Michael: last year she
went ashore on the narrows while leav
ing Vancouver, and now she is in trouble
again.

The Mail steamer City of Peking and the
transport Pennsylvania are being anx
iously looked for. The Vanderbllt people
want the Peking to come in, as Alfred
G. Vanderbllt is aboard, and a special
train has been in waiting ever since last
Friday on the Oakland mole to carry
him to New York. The Pennsylvania has
the Washington volunteers aboard, and a
delegation from Seattle, Tacoma and all
the principal Puget Sound ports has been
here nearly a week awaiting a chance to
welcome home the gallant boys from the
northern State. Friday, Saturday and
Sunday nights the Vanderbllt people have
had a tug in waiting to take the young
heir off the steamer as soon as she
dropped anchor, and should she get in to
day he will be rushed over to Oakland
and will go Hying East at express speed.
The naval hospital ship Solace, which
arrived from Yokohama Saturday night,
left one day after, the Peking and two
days after the Pennsylvania. She had
some heavy weather after leaving Yoko
hama, and it may. have been responsible

BACK FROM FROZEN NORTH.
Schooner Bonanza Returns From a
â Prisoners on the Rev
enue Cutter Sear.
ThÂ© schooner Bonanza arrived yester
day from Point Hope with 17,000 pounds
of whalebone, ten bundles of skins, fifteen
packages of furs and two packages of
ivory. Captain Smith reports leaving
Ponit Hope two days after the whaler
Mary D. Hume. He thinks he would have
beaten the latter down had it not been
for contrary winds. The Bear had two
prisoners, a white man and an Indian, on
board when she spoke the Bonanza. Both
men are accused of murder. The Indian
killed a gold hunter on Kotzebue Sound
for his provisions and the white man an
Indian chief during a quarrel. Both men
will be tried at Sitka.
The crew of the Bonanza had consider
able news of doings in Alaska, but it has
all been exploited by earlier arrivals.
The Pacinc Mail Company's steamer
San Jose arrived from Panama and way
ports yesterday morning and was placed
In quarantine. One of the Chinese pas
sengers was sick and, as yellow jack is
epidemic at Panama, the quarantine offi
cer ordered the vessel to Angel Island.
An examination showed the Mongolian to
be suffering from some simple ailment, so
the vessel was released and went to her
dock. The following cabin passengers
came up on the steamer: Franklin E.
Moore, Dr. R. Zaldwar, Max Rhode and
son, L. Wilhelmson, Robert Porter, Pearo
Tiexido, Jose Tonalla, Bruno E. Webber
and Chris Murkleson. The passengers re
port that lazarettos are being built at
Panama to accomodate the yellow fever
patients and that a strict quarantine is
being enforced on the isthmus.
Chinese Celebration Closes.
The festival of the Ghee Kung Tong
came to Its conclusion at an early hour
this morning. The closing and most im
posing ceremony of burning the josses
that have reigned In a profusion of splen
dor In the house on Spofford alley for a
year was performed at 4 o'clock this
morning, after which the sounds of rev
elry died, away, the cymbals ceased their
clanging and the regalia was stored away
in dry goods boxes to serve in the next
annual least of the family. Thousands of
people passed through Spofford alley last
night, visiting the josshouse and listening
to the weird services. The significance of
the event is the feeding of the needy rela
tives of the Ghee family.

MORNING GAME.
Sacramento 8, San Francisco 5.
The Initial morning game at the Recre
ation grounds between Sacramento and
San Francisco was anything but baseball,
and the score tells nothing of the listless
contest, that was more or lea/ a riot of
errors.
Fireman Fits was touched With streams
of hits in the early part of the game.
When the lire broke out in the hills he
steadied down, for the fear was in his
heart that he might be called upon to put
out the burning brush, as he could not
put out the Statesmen.
Doyle; for the Senators, was not mis
used, although Reiily caught one of his
curves In the bulge of his bat and the
ball went Bailing far away. it mounted
the center field fence, alio Reillv jogged
around the bags, the small boy on the
rocstlng bleachers touting an accompan
iment to his course.
In the third inning Fltzpatrick was
mauled, for three 'te, which, combined
with errors, resulted In four runs for the
Senators. These, added to the brace of
tallies In the first inning, gave Sacramento
a lead -which the locals could not over
come. In the fifth Sacramento scored two
runs.
For the locals Pyne scored in the sec
ond inning, after which nothing happened
until the eighth, when ffeldebrand Court
ney and Reiily crossed the rubber. In the
ninth Muller went to first on a hit scor
ing on Brockhoff's hit to far center. A
few pretty double plays were the only fea
tures of tiif game. The score:

AND CRABS LOSE TWO
OAKLAND, Oct. 8.-The Oaklands won
two games from the Santa Cruzans
to-day at Freeman's Park. The Sand
Crabs proved to be a bunch of lobsters.
The heat was intense and both sides ap
peared in had humor.
In the morning game Hutchinson of
Oaklands was the first man up. He hit
Pitcher helsn In the leg with a straight
liner, laying him up for the time. The
ball bounced off to the third baseman,
who threw it to first, putting Hutch out.
ihe I lay was a peculiar one. The only
other feature of the forenoon game was
Moskinmn home run bit for Oakland in
Sporting Stents from All Over the World.
the second Inning, when he brought
home Lange. and Smith. During the fifth
Inning, Devereaux of the lobsters proved
himself so obstreperous over a decision of
Umpire Graves, when the latter had called
Moskinian safe on first, that he had to be
tent to the bench and fined. The score
was as follows:

THE SAN -FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, OCTOBER 9, 1899.
OLYMPICS
WIN AGAIN
ON THE ROAD
Splendid Team Work Secures |
for Them a Magnificent |
Cycling Trophy.
The Olympic Club Wheelmen added
, fresh laurels to their road-racing fame
yesterday; by winning the annual twenty
mile event of the California Associated j
Cycling Clubs, run over the San Leandro j
triangle. 1
Yesterday's event was one of the four
big road races conducted by the associa- j
tion annually, and the trophy contested ]
for is the most valuable one offered, be- !
j ing a magnificent silver cup donated by
I Baker ft Hamilton. Yesterday was the I
j fourth time the race has been run. It j
I was won In 19.% by the Acme Club of
Oakland. In. 1887 the Bay city Wheelmen \
i took it. and last year the Olympics. The j
I cup has to be won three times by ono J
j club to become its personal property, and ;
I thus the Olympics have now obtained a j
j decided advantage.
A novelty in yesterday's race was the .
j Indiscriminate pacemaking allowed by a j
I new rule of the association. It proved as ,
j fair for one club as for the other, but j
i did not result in any broken records. The I
j Olympics' competitors were the Bay City â¢
I Wheelmen of this city and the Garden j
| City Wheelmen of San Jose, the tatter 1
club entering a team for the first time in
the history of the race. The Acmes ot I
1 Oakland did not put in a team this year. I
realizing, probably, that the Olympics'
men were almost unbeatable, a fact which
was predicted in yesterday's Call, and so
It proved. Careful training and splendid
pacing arrangements, added to the speed
of the riders themselves, did the trick,
although the result might have been dif
ferent, or a closer finish resulted, if the
Bay City man on the fourth relay. R. J.
Black, had not been overcome with heat
and fatigue and been obliged to quit when
I '.â¢â¢ race was half run, thus throwing his
team out- entirely.
The Garden Citys made an unexpectedly
good showing for their Brat attempt at
this race. They kept up about even on
the first relay, lost two minutes on the
second, but gained 8 minute in both the
third and fourth bunches. They lost the
race on the last lap by over two minutes,
their rider being unable to hold the Olym
pic man, E. A. Hozio.
Of the Olympians the greatest credit is
due to E. O. Kragness, who was their
first courier away. Me fell three times,
sustaining severe bodily injuries each
time, but came In bruised and bleeding
seven seconds ahead of the next man, ii
Bay City, and twenty-three seconds-ahead
of the Garden Cityite, His ride was truly
marvelous under the circumstances, and
showed wonderful grit and endurance.
Along with Kragness, equal honor Is due
Bozlo, who landed his club 2 minutes
22 3-5 seconds ahead at the finish, and
made the fastest ride of tho day, covering
the twenty miles In 52 minutes. 13 2-5 sec
onds, within two seconds of Kraft's rec
ord for the course, made in 1896. M. G.
Francis, Garden City, was next best In
point of speed, being 44 seconds slower
than Bozio.
The twenty-mile event of the association
is a curious one in its way, as it requires
each man on each team to ride the full
distance. Then the not riding time of the
live men from each club is added togeth
er, and the team riding the fastest In the
aggregate Is declared winner. In such a
sweltering hot day as it was yesterday at
San Leandro it is small wonder that many
of the men came In completely fagged out,
and one, as In Black's case, collapsed be
fore the finish was reached.
The race started at Fitchburg at 10:50
o'clock. Fitchburg is about three miles
this side of San Leandro. Taking the
county road to San Leandro (three miles),
the riders then traverse the famous San
Leandro triangular course (eight and a
half milts around!') twice, making the total
distance twenty miles. One man from
each team, making a group of three
riders, with their respective pacers in the
lead, started at the appointed time and
rode the full distance of twenty miles.
Another bunch followed them five minutes
later, and so on at Intervals of five min
utes until the five groups were away.
The following was the net riding time
of the three teams:
â¢Did not finish.
Fastest time. ISB9, E. A. Boslo, 52:13 2-B Ree.
ord. Charles A. Kraft, 1898, 62:11 2-5. '
Great credit, is. due Chairman, w, L.
Loos of the association racing board for
the excellent manner, in which the event
was managed, and he in turn modestly
blames it on the officials he appointed.
An immense crowd was at the finish, yet
the course was kept clear and the timers'
and judges' scores were accurate to a dot.
The Oylmpie Club entertained the win
ning team in an informal way at the club
house last evening, and -will banquet them
later on. The Bay Citvs, who are good
losers, but who have had a hard run of
luck lately, also held a jolification at their
home. There were dark hints thrown out
that perhaps their bulldog mascot.
"Mike. ' had survived his usefulness, and
some think that nothing but his demise
will shake off the- hoodoo.

Pacifies Defeat California Cricketers
With Three Wickets to Spare.

win the last scheduled match of the sea
son yesterday, being beaten by the Pacific

won the toss, and sent the scarlet and

TWO STAKES
FOR METALLIC
IN A MONTH
Pasha Kennels' Greyhound
Wins After Desperate Cours
ing at Union Park.
|Metalllc,the best of the greyhounds rep- I
resenting' Pasha kennels in the coursing
field, accounted for. another stake at
Union Coursing Park yesterday. Late In
the afternoon she had a desperate course
with Warship, In which the hare took
the dogs completely around the field. Both
dogs fell from exhaustion after the kill,
and it seemed impossible for Metallic to
recuperate sufficiently to come back
again. In the succeeding round she re
ceived an "accidental" bye, and again
was called upon In a severe course, the
hare being as fast as a bullet.
In the final Metallic met the Eastern
greyhound. Mountain Beauty, that had
been equally unfortunate throughout the
day. Three of her courses were "no goes,"
while the majority of them were long
ones. When wagering commenced on the
final. Mountain Beauty was made favorite
at odds of 5 i" 1. So much money was
offered on Metallic that the odds receded
rapidly to 3 to 1, at which figure most of
the money was placed. The llnal was run
at dusk and the result was never in doubt.
Metallic led from slips, and ran as though
It was her first time out. The Beauty had
not recuperated from her former efforts
and could not hold Metallic, which led
Up, turned and killed.
Firm Friend ran as consistently as ever,
working his way into the fifth round. He
was finally beaten by Whitehead, a most
promising youngster, by a score of 12 to
10. The Friend had all the better of the
early stages of the course, but the young
dog proved too much for him. In the
previous round he had a long "no go"
with Cavalier, whom he beat the second

VEREIN EINTRACHT PICNIC.
Big Crowd of Pleasure Seekers En
joy an Outing at Shell Mound.
The grand picnic and moonlight festival
of the San Francisco Verein Elntracht at
Shell Mound Park yesterday was a most
enjoyable occasion and a success. The
weather was perfect for a picnic and was
In a great measure responsible for the
large attendance, as everybody was anx
ious to get away from the heat of the city
and find a place where they could enjoy
a rest In the shade and at the same time
be within reach of an occasional glass of
beer. :-">"- ;-.;;
There were fully a thousand people,
voting and old. on the grounds, and they
were there for no other purpose than to
have a good time, .
The big pavilion was crowded all tne
afternoon and all the evening by dancers,
whose enthusiasm and endurance were
remarkable, considering the heat. Those
who did not feci equal to tripping the
light fantastic with the thermometer at
100 in the shade sat around In the arbors
or under the trees chatting, drinking beer.
swinging in -the hammocks, playing tne
wheel of fortune or indulging in some of
the various forms of sport provided for
their entertainment. It was midnight
when the- last boat-load of picnickers ar
rived on this side of the bay.
MANY GOLFERS OUT
ON SAN RAFAEL LINKS
Two Competitions Open to Both
Sexes Are Won by the
Ladies.
Mrs. R. A. Parker having presented
some handsome prizes to the San Rafael
Golf Club, to be competed . for by the
ladies, the competition took place last
Wednesday. The first prize was won by
Miss Wagner (handicap 25) with a gross
score of 86 and a net score of 61. and the
second prize by Mrs. J. T. Burke (handi
cap 12) with a gross score of 7S and a net
score of 86. The following ladles also en
tered the competition, which was at nine
holes: Mrs. J. J. Crooks. Miss Frances
Carroll. Mrs. R. Q. Brown, Mrs. Francis.
Mrs. de Young, Mrs. Curtis, Mrs. D. Don
ohoe, Mrs. F. S. Johnson. Mrs. F. 11.
Green, Miss Mary Me''ai!a and Miss T. C.
Morgan.
The third of the fortnightly tournament?
was held on Saturday, the principal event?
being a ladies' handicap at nine holes and
a men's handicap at eighteen holes. Play
in the ladies' handicap began at 10 o'clock
in the morning. The results are shown In
the following; table:

Mrs. H. G. "Brown and Miss Frances Car
roll tied for second prize. â '-'

in the afternoon. The results are herewith

icap a, and \V. Q. Curtis, handicap 22,
made no returns. In playing off the tie
Baron Alex yon Schroeder won first prize.
In thÂ» afternoon Instructor T. W. Tet
ley, to whose hard work is largely due the
enthusiasm shown by the members, in
stituted a novel contest In approaching
and driving, in which the ladies competed
with the men, there being a prize for each
department. The approach-putting con
test was won by Mrs. R. G. Brown, who
holed five balls from a distance of sev
enty-five yards In sixteen strikes. Lieu
tenant W. 6. Miller and J. J. 'rooks tied
Mrs. K. G. Brown's score, but in the play

In driving the clear carry of the men's

given the benefit of the roll. Here, too,

156 yards. The nearest to these excellent
drives was made by Baron J. H. yon
Bchroeder, whose longest ball had a clear

other competitors did not fall within the

YACHTSMEN MEET.
Annual Session of the Racing union
of North America.
NEW YORK, Oct. The annual meet
ing of the Yacht Racing Union of North
America was held last night in this city,
and the following officers were elected for
the ensuing year: Louis M. Clark, Bos
ton, chairman; Frank Browne Jones. New
York, secretary; Newbury D. Lawton,
New York, treasurer.
The matter of rescinding the g'rth meas-
I urement rule was brought to no definite
, conclusion. A motion authorising the
chair to appoint a committee to reviae the
racing laws was adopted, 'the council re
| ported .in favor of retaining the present
| rule of measurement, and a second com
j mittee was appointed to revise and com-
I plete the racing rules.
The associations represented at the
I meeting were: The Yacht Racing Associa
tion of Long Island Sound. Inland Lake
Yachting Association of Chicago. Inter
lake Yachting Association of Detroit,
Lake Racing Association of Toronto,
Yacht Racing Association of Boston, Nar
ragansett Bay Racing Association. New
York Yacht Racing Association; Pacific
: Interclub Yachting Association, Royal St.
I Lawrence Yacht Club of Montreal and the
; Atlantic, Larchrr.ont and Corinthian
' clubs. .
SUNDAY AT THE PARK.
Immense Crowds Take Advantage of
the Tropical Weather.
It was a had day for the doctors yester
day. The lame and the halt, rheumatics
and those afflicted with. any old 111, left
their beds by the thousands yesterday
and went forth into the glad sunshine
to get well. It Is estimated by Captain
Thompson of the Park Police that fully
40.000 people passed through the gates of
Golden Gate. Park yesterday. They
covered the walks and avenues from the
Baker-street entrance to the beach, and
down the line of breakers to the county
line.
in all litis crowd there was not a
serious accident reported. It was thought
that the extreme heat would prevent fast i
wheeling, but it seems that the scorcher
will not be put down, except by the
police, and thirteen cyclers were arrested
yesterday for violating the anti-scorch
ing ordinance. Those taken into custody
are 11. Mlcsner. S. Yoshikai, William
Roberts, A. Engwer, P. Altmeyer, F.
Tlckner. William Hoffman. K. Price. C.
Smith, A. Logic. William Moore, F. Gold
berg and R. Miller.
The crowd at the Chutes was also very
large. "Prtncess," the big elephant, was
the attraction of the day, but the giant
Galapagos from the South Sea Islands
also received considerable attention.
Next week Major Illte, the midget, who.
It is said, will marry Chlqulta at the
Paris fair, will entertain the audiences in i
the pavilion.

Saloon-Keepar Stabbed.
Charles Manning, ' a saloon-keeper at
1055 Mission street, was stabbed in the
hip with a pocketknlfe last night by
Elmer Miller, who was arested by Officers
Eskew and Orr and booked at the City
Prison for assault with a deadly weapon.
Miller entered Manning's saloon early in
the evening, and becoming boisterous was
ordered out. He refused to go, and when
Manning tried to eject him Miller plunged
the blade of a pocketknlfe Into the sa
loonman's hip.
Arrested the Woman.
Allle Keefe, a young woman, was ar
rested yesterday at Petaluma by Detec
tives Ryan and ODea of this city and
brought to the City Prison, where she
was charged jointly with Joseph Jones,
alias Silve, a former bellboy at the Win
chester on Third street, with grand lar
: ceny. They are accused of having drug
i ged and robbed Edward Jones of Custer
I County, Idaho, of three gold watches.

6tS Market at., opp. Palac, Hotel, San FranciscoALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
MEN OF TRADE
TO CONFER ON
CIVIC PROGRESS
Require New Blood
in New Oakland.
Oakland Olfico San Francisco Call.
'.'OS Broadway, Oct. 8.
The Merchants' Exchange has called an
open conference for next Tuesday even
ing to consider "ways and means for se
curing new industries for Oakland." The
idea so often referred to Ii these columns
that one enterprising merchant la worth
to a community a herd of politicians is
:-.ow forcing itself home to those who
have the commercial interests of the city ,
at art. It is now realized more than
ever before that the welfare of Oakland
has been retarded by selfish politicians,
who have not hesitated to use every
means to further their own ambitions. !
This has at last become so notorious that
outside municipalities have noticed it. and i
comparisons have been made which have ''
awakened local merchants. Nearly every- i
thing for the past twenty years has been j
dominated by politics. The letting of a
contract, no matter how small or how 1
large; the efforts to acquire water front
and to determine its ownership; the . na- I
tion of a Postofflce site; all manner of'
publio Improvements; and In fact the
whole business of the municipality, boTn
that which concerns the government It- I
self and also that which controls local
progress, has been handled by politicians
In their own interests.
On Tuesday evening It is expected that i
soma plain truths will be told, and that ',
something more material and productive i
will be accomplished than the passing of j
resolutions. Oakland has been made ridic- i
ulous by resolutions. At the drop of the
hat resolutions can be framed and passed j
regulating; everything from the construe- '.
tion of the Nicaragua canal to the con- j
duct of affairs In the Philippine Islands.
This resolution business has been largely |
the means by which politicians have j
worked and have sought to give an offi- ;
cial significance to actions largely per- :
Bona!. This has not built factories nor j
filled the city with worklngmen and â¢
their families, which are the inly back
bone around which a progressive city cart
be raised.
In a message to the Council forty-five
years ago Mayor pentler said; "There
are, unfortunately, persons in every com
munity not particularly distinguished for
enterprise or attention to their own busi
ness, who are always eager to agitate
and embroil under the hope that out of
the confusion there will come spoils."
If the Merchants' Exchange at the com
ing confert nee compile a list of those
people whs come within the meaning of
Horace Cwrpentier's definition and takes
measures ii suppress them, it will have
found 0r.6 answer to Its question of
"How to provide ways and means for se
curing new industries for Oakland."
A few days ago Gideon Davis decided
that it was time to give the. city a shak
ing up. HÂ« notice- Supervisor Mitchell
walking languidly down the street, think
ing of the possibility of another nomina
tion; he recollected hat since April 1 ex-
Mayor W. R. Thomas had been In Be
clusion; ne remembered that since the
Oakland Exposition Theo. Gler had not
been heard of; and as his mind wandered
over these thoughts Gideon decided that
he would, just fur the fun of it, have all
these people and a great many more
chasing each other and waking up the
town fore many days had passed.
Nor was there any difficulty in devising
the means to set the town jumping. Gid
eon remembered that in the summer of
last year the Montana troops came to
Oakland and amused" Its Citizens by shoot
ing blank cartridges and perspiring for
several hours on a hot Fourth of July on
the Piedmont hills. He took his cue from
this recollection; and forthwith sat down
and wrote an article that stirred up all
the patriotism and sense of gratitude in
the city. lie decided that the Montana
men should have a banquet; not only a

"It is planned to give a diploma which
shall be a valuable credential, and per
haps In the fututre the apron and cap to
which our servants so sturdily object may
not be a badge Of servitude, but a sign
of honorable labor."
This remark la part of an expression of
the opinion of the leading ladies of Oak
land regarding the foundation of a school
of housekeeping, which it is proposed to
establish in this city. The plaint Is old
that for the last ten years the servant
question needs adjusting. The paragraph
quoted seems to me to suggest not only
the way out of the difficulty but the way
m. If. as is stated, the apron and cap
have been hitherto "a badge of servi
tude," then no other explanation Is neces
sity for the existence of the alleged dif
ficulty. Slaves will never rest easily under
the yoke in this country, and as each year
passes and the standard of education is
raised it naturally follows that the wear
ers of the apron and cap will become less
and less reconciled to servitude, but will
demand that their duties be classed as
"honorable labor," with all the privileges
and appurtenances thereunto belonging.
History as well as every day experience
would seem to prove that the day of the
lackey, who was expected tO bow tO the
floor every time his lord passed, and of
the serving woman, who curtesied to the
lady as she gave her orders for dinner,
passed with the old monarchical ideas,
and that in a democracy dally becoming
more democratic It is both silly and use
less to talk of "badges of servitude." No
precedent can be put forward for the
treatment of servants in modern Ameri
can domestic life, and although "schools
of domestic science" and institutions for
promulgating the ethics of housekeeping
may be very good in their way, it would
seem that the only manner to meet the
"servant's difficulty" is by the exercise of
good American horse sense.
In this connection there are a few other
thoughts that suggest themselves. Ser- |
vants, that is of the "badge of servitude"
kind, cannot be blamed for outgrowing
their conditions of servitude in a country
where nearly every day one of the scions
of the house elopes with one of the lady
employes or where the lady herself runs
Off with the butler or the coachman. Such
examples must be considered in the treat
ment of this question. Although much ef- ,
fort has been made during the past
decade to adjust the servant question. I
have not yet heard of any convention In
which the servants were invited to pre- .
sent their side of the interesting situa
tion.
Americanism is a peculiar thing. It la
responsible for the fact that a canal boy
or a rail splitter may become President, i
An Individual may start out in life by be- I
ing a servant, but it would be a fool who
would prophesy to what height in civil or
Official life such a sen-ant may attain. I
am afraid the servant question will bother
us for some time to come. ;

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, OCTOBER 9, 1899.
QUEER TURN IN
MONTGOMERY
DIVORCE SUIT
Husband Decides Not
to Contest.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Oct. 8.
The divorce suit brought recently by
Jennie Montgomery against "Jack" Mont
gomery on the ground of extreme cruelty
will be robbed of Its erstwhile promised
sensational features.
The defendant has determined not to
contest the suit and hence his default for
failure to appear or answer the complaint
was entered several days ago and the
case referred to Court Commissioner Bab
cock, who yesterday took the testimony
of Mrs. Montgomery. The wife's attor
neys, Messrs Garrity and Donovan, were
present, but the defendant was not rep
resented.
It had been supposed that Montgomery
would file a cross-complaint setting forth
as onÂ« of his most weighty allegations
the sensational occurrence at his home
one night some months ago, when his
wife, after a scene in a West Oakland res
taurant where she took him by surprise
while he was In the company of another
woman, is alleged to have dashed some
carbolic acid into his face, nearly causing
him to lose his eyesight.. It had also been
expected that Mrs. Montgomery's testi
mony would in Itself prove spicy and sen
sational in view of the cruelty charges,
but this expectation, too, was not real
ized, and according to her own words
given yesterday under oath the husband
on no occasion used any particular vio
lence on her during the six years of their
married life.
Her sole cause for complaint. it appears.
lay In her testimony to the effect that
her erring spouse remained away from
home of nights, not Infrequently basking
In the smiles of other women. It Is ex
pected that Court Commissioner Babeock
will report on the case early this week.
If marriage makes one. of two it must
be a continuation of single blessedness.
The earlier the bird catches the worm
the longer he will have to wait for his
noonday lunch.

grees and had not been below 90 up to
5:30 p. m.
"I am at a loss to explain the cause
of this freaky weather. This morning
it was 20 decrees warmer at San Fran
cisco than at Point Reyes, and the
temperature in the valleys was much
cooler than here. I think that wo will
have another day of hot weather at
least. I can attribute this spell to
some peculiar condition in the North
Pacific Ocean, the cause of which can
not now be explained."
There have been several unofficial
reasons given for this extraordinary
warm spell. One is to the effect that a
bunch of Jasper McDonald's rulings
escaped from Native Sons' Hall and
distributed themselves about the city.
South of Market politicians, however,
claim that the molten lava that es
caped from Jim Ncal last Friday night
at tho Democratic Convention has
heated the atmosphere.

CUPID CRUSHED
BY A STUBBORN
ALAMEDA MAMA
End of Roebke-Green
Romance.
ALAMEDA, Oct. B.â The romantic love
affair of Miss Amanda Roebke and Dr.
Jacob S. Green is at an end. Cupid has
been completely overthrown. The odds
against the little god was too great; his
golden darts and his tiny bow were no
weapons to win a victory against tne
stern commands of an angry mamma
and the spirit of a young lady who re
fused to be trifled with.
The Roebke-Green romance furnished
food for the gossips of the Encinal dur
ing all of last February. The engagement
of the young people was announced after
the doctor had passed through the Golden
Gate on his way to Acapulco, where he
went as surgeon on the steamer City of
Panama. No sooner had the news been
published that the medico had wooed and
won the fair brunette than Mrs. J. S.
Green, mother of the prospective groom,
rushed into print and in a most emphatic
manner denied that there was any truth
1- the engagement announcement, and
supplemented her statement with the
threat that if her son was engaged in a
love affair she knew nothing about she
would frustrate his plans just as soon as
he returned from the isthmus.
Cupid held his own during this part of
the controversy, and appearances indi
cated that he would win out at the finish.
Miss Roebke wired to her sweetheart
about the attitude of Mamma Green, and
her affianced wrote in reply that he was
hers till death, and that mamma nor
anybody else could "cut their love In
two."
The end of all this pretty tale Is that
Miss Roebke In a fit of indignation has
hurled her engagement ring into the face
of her affianced, and now it is all off.
Dr. Green returned from the Isthmus a
few days ago. As soon as he reached
town he called upon Miss Roebke. Their
meeting was affectionate, and their pro
testations of everlasting love were re
peated over and over again. Mamma
Green was not consultedâ she . did not
even know that her boy was back. The
happy young couple hired a horse and
buggy and drove about the city just to
let the gossips know that the stories of
their engagement were true.
The climax came the following day.
Dr. Green had visited his mother in the
meantime, and when he called upon Miss
Roebke there was sadness In his face and
sorrow in his voice. Tearfully he in
formed her that their engagement would
have to be called off; he had spoken to
his mother about It, and he was forced
to tell his sweetheart that if - their, wed
ding took place it would break the old
lady's heart.
Miss Roebke wasted no words. She
said that she would not have a man for
a husband who did not have nerve
enough to choose a wife for himself with
out letting his mother interfere. From
her finger she pulled the golden band
that Dr. Green had given her In ex
change for her promise to be his and,
summoning all her strength, threw it In
In his face. She made him pick it up from
the floor, where it had fallen, and then
told him to leave her presence and never
to cross her path again.
The gossips are busy once more and
Cupid is weeping.
