GENERAL FUND EXHAUSTED.
ALAMEDA, Oct. Bâ The city general
fund became practically exhausted yester
day, there being but $25 in the fund.
There happened to be a few surplus dol
lars in the electric light fund, and the
general fund was made richer to the
amount of $S0 by drawing on that fund.
The last fiscal year ended in July of
this year, and the city has been running
since that time on an excessive tax rate
apportionment from last year. Salaries,
etc., for September have been paid, but
there now remains but $105 in the general
fund to pay bills that may have been pre
sented since that time.
The Trustees fixed the tax levy for this
year at their last meeting, but as the
taxes do not become delinquent until De
cember it may be nearly two months be
fore the city has sufficient money to pay
all October demands against the general
fund. The salaries of the majority of the
city officials and employes are paid out
of this fund.
Death of Sam S. Harvey.
OAKLAND, Oct. Chief Deputy Dis
trict Attorney Melvin received news to
day of the death of his cousin, Samuel
Slemmons Harvey, which occurred yes
terday at Truckee. Death is attributed to
typhoid fever. Deceased was aged 31
years and was the son of C. D. Harvey,
United States Commissioner of Bankrupt
cy, residing at Loomis, Placer County.
He was employed as a railway engineer
running between Truckee and Wads
worth. Deceased was a former resident
of Oakland and was educated here.
Searched for Remains.
OAKLAND, Oct. B.â A diligent search
has been conducted all day in the ruins
of the hotel which was practically burned
to the ground at Lafayette yesterday,
but the remains of Joseph Cooper, the
proprietor, and his cook, who were sup
posed to have met death In the flames,
have not been discovered.
The belief prevails now that Cooper and
his cook were at San Francisco at the
time of the fire. Nothing has been heard
from either of them.
SORRY THAT HE SPOKE.
Mayor Phelan Apologizes for His
Hasty Reference to Horace
Davis.
At the Democratic convention Friday
night Mayor Phelan, during the course of
his address, referred to Horace Davis, the
Republican nominee, as a traitor to the
city. The Mayor states now that the
| words, while expressing the idea that he
i intended to convey, were more harsh than
j he thought at the moment, and he en
deavors to make amends for his haste in
I the following open letter addressed to Mr.
Davis:
SAN FRANCISCO, Oct. 8, IS?9.
Mr. Horace Davisâ Dear Sir: My impulse
to write you Is so strong that 1 take this
opportunity to say that In making an Im
promptu address Friday night before the
convention I used a wordâ first that
came to my lipsâ which 1 at once regretted;
and while expressive of my idea, it was
too harsh a term to apply to a gentleman
who has always bees courteous to me and
whose character I have always respected.
I would, therefore, modify It anil will now
take public occasion to do so. The Intro
duction of national politics In this cam
paign I hold Is repugnant to the charter,
cue of whose principal purposes is to give
all our citizens, unmoved by mere partisan
bias, an opportunity of passing upon the
conduct of their local affairs and candi
dates.
Therefore separate municipal elections
at considerable cost have been ordered. If
your contention be true, the charter has
erred; but we know that' bad municipal gov
ernment In San Francisco has come largely
from the fact that unfit men have lloated
Into office under party pressure or under
the shadow of greater and more exciting
Issues than local Interests can present. Bui
Concerning the purpose of the charter there
can be no dispute, because it further pro
vides for the appointment of bi-partisan
boards, neither party having the advantage,
end takes the clerkships out of the cate
gory of patronage by Introducing the merit
system of public employment under civil
service rules.
It has been often said, and it is no doubt
true, that In appointing members of hoard*,
as under the charter, there is a greater
danger of making enemies than of hold
ing friends. On the other hand. I am free
to admit that by appointing good boards Ir
respective of party, as provided for, the ap
pointing power may make friends of the
community and thus rrflexlvely benefit the
organization for which he stnnds.
But I am sure as a citizen you would
net deprecate this incentive tO faithful ser
vices. In the home field: nor as a partisan
believe that "ueftlons of currency and tariffs
would be affected by focal success at pure
ly municipal elections. Our people, you
must admit. are certainly intelligent
enough to discriminate between a local plat
form and a national platform, without re
ared to Individuals and will vote accord
ingly when the Issue* are made.
I therefore as a citizen ree-ret that yon
should have made your statement that this
election shall now and here embrace 'he
larger questions of national policy. Itâ¢
you could consistently with the charter
have arrived at that conclusion I do not
know, and If your action was deliberate I
believe you arc wrong.
But you may have been as Impulsive In
deed as I, when by an unhappy expression
I rather rudely characterized your position:
and for which you will please, accept my
mature regrets.
I trust our otherwise friendly relations
may not In any way be prejudiced by what
has been said. Tours truly,

DISEASE FINDS
A NEW VICTIM
AMONG NORSES
Miss Plummer Sent
to Quarantine.
â Â»
TENDED SMALLPOX PATIENT
â¢
WAS ACCOMPANIED BY MISS
PAGE, ALSO A NURSE.
m
Courts-Martial for All at the Camp
of the Isolated Thirty-First.
Freedom Again De
layed.
The smallpox at the Presidio will not
I down. Cases have been developing right
j along, and every time the surgeons
j breathe easier and believe they have seen
the last of the disease it comes up again
lln a new place. The last victim Is Mn>s
Plummer, a nurse at the general hospital,
who was taken down with the smallpox
yesterday.
Miss Plummer was a nurse in ward G.
She has had a great many patients under
her care, and among them have oeen two
who found their way out to the detention
| camp on the hills of the Presidio. She
I was vaccinated twice recently, but in
neither case was it effective. She became
l sick three days ago, and since then she
â  has done no duty, and yesterday morning
; the symptoms became unmistakable.
Miss Plummer was sent out to the de
! tention camp yesterday afternoon. All
i her belongings in the hospital were fu
migated, and those who were in her im
' mediate company have been Isolated and
I vaccinated. Miss Page, another nurse
! from the hospital, who has had the small-
I pox, went out to the detention camp with
i her, but she will not be able to take care
!of her patient without assistance. It is
â¢ therefore thought that another nurse will
i have to go out to-day or to-morrow.
i Luckily Miss Plummer's case is not a seri
ous one, and she is not considered in
' much danger, but the disease is as in
; fectious in its lighter stages as it is when
j it becomes more virulent, so the risk v
I Just as great for all who have to ccme In
i contact with the patient. The other pa
â  teints at the camp, suspects as well as
| sick, are reported to be unchanged in
: their condition. â¢ __ .
It was expected that the luckless Thir
' tv-first regiment would be released from
j quarantine to-day, but it is again a case
iof hope deferred. There are still a couple
I of suspicious cases, and not until they
1 have shown themselves to be harmless
will the command be allowed its freedom.
j This will be about the 14th, that is, unless
i the suspected cases develop into the genu
ine disease. In that event two weeks
i more is the least the regiment may ex-
i Pect.
Even if released from quarantine the
' regiment will not leave the island until the
I transport that will take it to Manila is
1 ready, and then the men will be taken
dlrectlv on board from the camp.
Reports of the recent doings of the
command are hard to get, owing to the
rigid quarantine, so but little more is
â¢ known of the shooting affair and the es
capade of the two officers who sighed for
| companionship. It was reported from the
I office of the adjutant of the regiment yes
i terday, however, that the shooting af
fray was not the result of any feud, but
! just the consequences of a big man going
I after a little man who had a rifle handy.
I It was a private quarrel, and. the little
1 man, seeing a drubbing in sight, called
i his rifle to his aid and punctured his
heavier opponent in the arm and leg. In
anticipation of the expected departure of
the regiment, no time was lost In prepar
ing to heal the breach in outraged mili
tary law, and a general court-martial
was accordingly appointed to meet this
morning. The detail for the court is as
follows:
Major Hunter Liggett, Captain ;
Charles P. Stivers, Captain Paul C.
Galleher, Captain Ellison L. Gilmer,
First Lieutenant Arthur T. Balentine,
First Lieutenant .William M. Meek,
First Lieutenant Perc H. Hawkins,
Second Lieutenant Watson Lindsey,
Second Lieutenant William B. Eulass,
First Lieutenant Willliam H. Monroe,
judge advocate. All are of the Thirty
first regiment.
As the command Is in quarantine none
but officers of the Thirty-first could be
i placed on the court. It is also stated in
i the order that the court is empowered to
try all prisoners who may be brought be
â  fore it, a clause that may be of interest to
! the two lonely officers, for General Shatter
says if there has been any attempt to
' break the quarantine there will be trouble
for the offenders. It is reported, however,
that the two officers were trying to rescue
i a boat in distress, an act of humanity
i to which no blame can attach, and there
was no doubt that after the plug had been
kicked out the boat was in distress, but
whether or not this will offset the pres-
I ence of the boat is a matter for future
1 settlement.
Colonel Jacob B. Rawles. Third Artll
lerv, with the headquarters staff, band
I and Batteries C, E. I and O and one-half
each of A and N, with Troop G of the
; Sixth Cavalry, have been ordered to be In
: readiness to escort the Kansas and Wash
! ington regiments from the transport dock
to the Presidio. The order calls for an
assembly at the Folsom street wharf at
i 9:30 o'clock the morning of the debarking
of the troops. It was made In anticipation
of the arrival of the regiments yesterday.
! but it holds for any time they may come
â  in.
Captain Edgar S. Walker, Eighth In
fantry, has been ordered to proceed to
Fort Snelling, Minnesota, for duty with
the depot battalion of his regiment.

Berkeley News Notes.
BERKELEY, Oct. The Board of
Trustees will hold their regular meeting
In the Town Hall to-morrow night at 8
o'clock. It is expected that Superintend
ent of Streets Guy Hyde Chick will tender
his resignation, and C. Maloney will be
appointed in his stead.
The Berkeley Republican Club will meet
In Golden Sheaf Hall next Tuesday even
ing at 8 o'clock. A meeting of the execu
tive committee has been called for to
morrow evening at the same hour In D.
J. Tobin's office, at Stanford place.

GOLDEN JUBILEE
OF THE CHURCH
OF ST. FRANCIS
Impressive Services
Mark the Event.
â â¦ â
BUILDING IN A NEW GARB
Â»
DONS GAY ATTIRE FOR. THE
NOTABLE OCCASION.
m
For Fifty Years It Has Been a Land
mark and Many Prominent
Priests Have Occupied Its
Pulpit. *
The closing services of the celebration
attending the commemoration of the
golden jubilee of St. Francis* Roman
Catholic Church, Vallejo street, took
place yesterday afternoon and evening.
The services were the most notable In the
history of the Catholic church in this
cityâ indeed, St. Francis Church is a part
of the city's history.
The celebrant of the jubilee mass was
Rev. J. Valentine of Sausalito; deacon.
Rev. P. Cummings of St. Patrick's; sub
deacon. Rev. Father Augustine of St.
Boniface's Church. The sermon was
preached by Very Rev. J. J. Prendergast
of St. Mary's Cathedral, and the closing
remarks in the evening was made by Rev.
Father Sasia, who spoke of "The Catholic
Church as God's Masterpiece on Earth.'
The members of the Young Men's Cath
olic Union, with Mayor Phelan, attended
in a body. ' ,'- . ' .. '
The music was especially good, the
"Kyrie," "Gloria," "Sanctus" and "Ag
nus Dei" was sung, as well as the
"Credo" and "Benedictus" from Webber,
in B flat ,â¢â¢ â _
The soloists were: Sopranos, Mrs. B. F.
Sides and Miss Agnes Devlin; contraltos.
Miss Valorton, Miss Margaret Brougham
and Mrs. John Sheerin: tenors, Thomas
Walsh, L. Anderson and Mrs. T. Hlllard:
bassos, Messrs. J. C. Woodbridge and
Lane: also a chorus of fifty voices. Miss
Maude Fay sang Wiegand's "O Salu
tarls," with violin obligato by Miss Clo
tilde Devlin. Mrs. A. B. Collins presided
at the organ. â ? : â ,
In the evening at 7:30 o'clock Lejeal 3
solemn vespers were sung, with Miss Ltl
lie Roeder, Miss Walsh, Mrs. A. B. Col
lins, Miss Ella McCloskey, as soprano
soloists, and Messrs. Walsh and Hlllard,
tenor soloists; and J. C. Woodbridge,
basso.
Through the energies of Rev. Father
Caraher, the weather-worn appearance of
the old red brick structure, which has
been a landmark of the city for the past
flftv years, was completely changed. The
outer walls have been given a coating of
plaster of granite shade. The Interior
of the edifice for yesterday's celebration
was a work of art. The decorations were
in gold with a slight relief of pure white.
The altar and organ loft were decorated
with natural flowers in great profusion.
The founders of St. Francis' Church
were Father Magognotte. affectionately
known as "Father Peter." who in course
of time became vicar general, and Father
Blalve. the organizer of the French con
gregation. After him at St. Francis' came
Rev. Eugene O'Connell, who was later
consecrated Bishop of Marysvllle; Fathers
T. Quinn. Timasio, Kenny. Fortnick and
Accolti, S. J., and Maraschl, S. J., who
eventually labored together zealously in
building up St. Ignatius College and
Church on the block of land on which the
Parrott building is now situated. Then
came Fathers King of Oakland,
Macken, Sanchez. Carroll, Florlan and
Thomas Cian, the Chinese priest, who
pursued his ecclesiastical studies in China
and subsequently in Rome. He spent sev
eral years as assistant pastor of St.
Francis' Church. Following were Fathers
Vila, Raho, Auge, Cupln, Cotter, Gabriel,
Serrano, Aerden, O. P.; Vincent Vinljes,
O P.: Garnga, F. Valentin!. Serda. T.
O'Nell, O. P.: John Harrington, A. Gar
riga. James O'Connor. P. Duffy, P. Smith,
Fassonotti, Melvin. Dillon, Larkin. J. Cor
coran, J. G. Conlan, H. K. White. W.
O'Grady. T. B. Duffy. M. J. Giles, P. Hes
lin and T. Caraher, the present pastor.
LECTURE ON CREMATION
Prank B. Ixibson Addresses the
Members of the Social Dem
ocrat Party.
Frank B. Gibson, the manager of the
crematory at the Cypress Lawn Ceme
tery, delivered an interesting: lecture to
the Social Democrat party at the temple
on Turk street yesterday evening. His
subject was "Cremation," and his ad
dress was illustrated by stereoptlcon
views. The speaker gave many interest
ing statistics during the course of his ad
dress. He stated that cremation is rap
idly coming into favor, especially in San
Francisco, the number of cremations for
the present year averaging forty per
month.
LEVY SAMUELS DIED
SUDDENLY YESTERDAY
Rumors of Suicide Scoffed At by
Relatives Who Found Him
Suffering.
OAKLAND, Oct. B.â Levy Samuels of
the firm of Peterson & Samuels, proprie
tors of Juanita stables at Seven
teenth street and San Pablo avenue, died
very suddenly at the home of his daugh
ter, Mrs. A. F. Peterson, 1422 Myrtle
street, shortly after 7 o'clock this morn-
Samuels was found in bed suffering
great pain when Mr. Peterson went to
arouse him for the morning meal. A phy
sician was immediately summoned, but
before he could arrive Samuels had passed
The cause of Mr. Samuels' death is un
known and an autopsy and inquest has
been ordered by Coroner Mehrmann, to be
held to-morrow evening. Deceased had
not previously complained of illness, and
up to the time of retiring last night ap
peared in the best of health and good
spirits. , ,
A rumor had gained circulation to thÂ«
effect that Samuels had committed sui
cide, but this the relatives emphatically
deny, and, moreover, no poison or other
indication in that direction was found.
Deceased was a native of Austria, aged 69
years, and leaves several grown up chil
dren.
Boy Crushed Under Car-wheels.
OAKLAND. Oct. Willie Anderson,
aged 10 years. residing with his parents
at 1725 Fifteenth street, attempted this
afternoon to board a moving freight train
at West Oakland Point and was run over,
the wheels crushing both feet. He was
removed to the receiving hospital and to
night Drs. Porter and Buteau amputated
his legs below the knees.

Weather Report.
(120 th Meridianâ Time.)
SAN FRANCISCO, Oct. 8, 5 p. m.
The following maximum temperatures were J
reported from stations in California to-day:
Eureka 58 Han Diego 92
Fresno 90 Sacramento 94
Los Angeles 100 j Independence 80
Red Bluff 91 Yuma 86
San Luis Obispo.... 96|
San Francisco data: Maximum temperature,
94; minimum, 66; mean, 75.
WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
Unusual weather conditions prevail over Cali
fornia to-night. A temperature of SO degrees
Is reported at San Francisco, with a maximum
of 93.9. Maximum temperatures exceeding 90
degrees, and in some Instances reaching 100.
are reported. From San Diego to San Fran
cisco there has been a rise of 10 to 18 degrees.
An area of high pressure has appeared sud
denly along the coast from Eureka to Neah, j
displacing the storm central oft the Washing
ton coast last night.
The following maximum wind velocities are
reported: Eureka, 30 miles per hour from the
northwest; Spokane, 28 west: Pocatelio, 36
west; Yuma, 28 northwest; Red Bluff, 30 from
the north. A moderate norther Is blowing in
the Sacramento Valley.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight October 9. 1898:
For Northern Californiaâ Monday,
with conditions favorable for showers Monday
night; northerly winds in the valleys; south
westerly winds on the coast; cooler.
For Southern Californiaâ Cloudy Monday;
cooler Monday night; northerly, changing to
southwesterly winds.
For Nevadaâ Monday, with showers;
cooler in northern portion.
For Cloudy Monday; probably showers
Monday night.
For Arizonaâ Fair Monday.
For San Francisco and vicinityâ Cloudy Mon
day: cooler; possibly showers at night; fresh
westerly winds.
ALEXANDER C. McADIE. I
Forecast Official. '

United States Coast and Geodetic Survey-
Times and Heights of High and Low
Waters at Fort Point, entrance to San
Francisco Bay. Published by official au
thority of the Superintendent.
NOTEâ The high and lew waters occur at
the city front (Mission-street wharf) about
twenty-five minutes later than at Fort Point;
the height of tide Is the same at both places.
NOTEâ In the Ã¼bove exposition of the tides j
the early morning tides are given In the left I
hand column and the successive tides of the \
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
'he day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are In addition to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign ( â ) precedes the height,
and then th* number given la subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

HIS COMMISSION FOUND
AMONG A LOT OF ASHES
Startling Discovery by Major
O'Neil of the First California.
The Document Promoting Second Lieuten
ant Wall, Signed by Governor Gage,
Unearthed in a Barrel Back of
the Occidental Hotel.
THROUGH the blundering methods j
of Governor Gage and Adjutant
General Seamans, and the criminal
act of somebody who Is yet un
identified, William Wall, a brave
officer of the First California Regi
ment has been deprived of his commis
sion as first lieutenant of the regiment.
While ranking as second lieutenant of
the regiment, as he and his fellow
eoldlers thought, Wall should have
.drawn pay as first lieutenant according
' to the commission issued to him by the
Governor, and which was found yester
day hidden in an ash barrel in the Oc
cidental Hotel. The Important discovery
' was made by Major O'Neil of the "Fight
ing First."
The story of the loss of the commission
dates back several months ago. There
was a hot fight on among the friends of
the officers of the regiment to Bee who
would receive recognition at the hands
of the Governor for meritorious service
at Manila.
Wall was strongly indorsed for the
first lieutenancy of the regiment on ac
: count of the bravery he displayed in the
recent unpleasantness with Spain. He
had a strong rival In Arthur Rivers,
who had been accused of cowardice by
Major O'Neil, but who was acquitted of
the charge after an Investigation con
ducted by Colonel Smith. Wall finally
captured the prize, the commission being
duly made out by the chief executive
and sent to Adjutant General Seamans,
â¢who was stopping at the Occidental
Hotel. Rivers was commissioned second
lieutenant, much to the surprise of his
friends, who expected he would outrank
Wall In the fight for the first lieutenancy
of the regiment.
While Wall was being congratulated on
his promotion, word was received that a
mistake had been made and that Rivers
had been promoted to the rank of first
lieutenant and Wall to the second lieu
tenancy. It was claimed at the time that
Wall's commission as first lieutenant
had not been signed by the Governor,
who at the last moment concluded to give
the place to Rivers. Suspecting some
thing wrong Major O'Neil went to
Colonel Duboce and showed him a tele
gram from Secretary of State Curry, In
forming him that Wall had been com
missioned first lieutenant of the regiment.
Duboce told him that a mistake had been
committed and that Rivers would be
recognized as the first lieutenant.
Major O'Neil the following day visited '
the adjutant general in the Ocidental ;
Hotel and demanded an explanation. Sea
mans treated him indifferently, but when
questioned by O'Neil he vociferously de
clared that the Governor had never
thought of promoting Wall to the rank
of first lieutenant Determined to ascer
tain the truth of this assertion, O'Neil
made a quiet investigation with the re- ;
sult that he learned that the commission
making Wall first lieutenant of the reg
iment had not only been signed by the
Governor, but had been forwarded to this
city. He also learned that the document
had not been canceled and that the ene- â .
mies of his friend Wall were making
frantic efforts to get possession of it.
In some manner, which he refused to i
discuss. O'Neil learned that the commis- !
sion was hidden in an ash barrel In the
rear of the Occidental Hotel, and he de- !
termined to get possession of it in the
hope of disproving Seamans" statement.
that the Governor had not signed a com
mission promoting Wall to the rank of
first lieutenant of the California regiment.
On going to the hotel O'Neil took a bell
boy into his confidence and Induced him
to show him the ash barrels. After ex
amining two of them he finally found the
commission secreted under a pile of ashes. :
With many expressions of Joy O'Neil,
after closely inspecting the document,

placed it in his pocket and left the hotel.
Subsequently he turned It over to Wall,
after advising him to keep it as an evi
dence of treachery on the part of the Gov
ernor and Adjutant General Seamans.
To-day O'Neil intends to Interview the
Governor with a view of finding out why
the commission of Wall had not been
honored and primarily to Induce him to
make an Investigation into the finding of
the document In the ash barrel, and thus
learn the name of the person who so
disposed of it.
When seen yesterday Wall said that
after ho had received his commission as
second lieutenant of the regiment he
learned that the Governor had also signed
another commision promoting him to the
rank of first lieutenant, lie was at a loss
LIEUT. WALL'S COMMISSION FOUND IN AN ASH BARREL
to explain why the document had been
thrown in an ash barrel, except that his
enemies were anxious to "bury It," as he
expressed it.

PREFERRED DEATH TO JAIL
George Davis, Serving Time in the
County Jail, Attempts Suicide.
One of the most ghastly sights imagina
ble was presented to the view of Drs. T.
J. Crowley and P. Stephens of the City
and County Hospital yesterday morning
when George Davis, a prisoner from the
BIG FIRE IN THE TREES
NEAR GOLDEN GATE PARK
A FIERCE fire in a forest of gum
trees and underbrush that for a
time baffled the efforts of the
Fire Department and threatened
the destruction of several small
ranch houses, ragod yesterday after
noon In the hills south of the Affili
ated Colleges. A largo area was
burned over before the Are ran Its
course. The fact that there was not a
breath of wind blowing is the only
thing that saved the western part of
the city from serious damage.
About 11 o'clock a little puff of
smoke rose up from a ravine back of
the colleges and curled In a column
straight into the air. It attracted the
attention of residents of the neighbor
hood, who, knowing the Inflammable
character of the underbrush, notified
the men- of engine 26, who responded
promptly to a still alarm.
While the firemen were on their way
to the fire, flames burst from the ra
vine, {riving the surrounding hills the
appearance of a volcano in active
eruption. Tongues of Are leaped into
the air, end a cloud of smoke spread
like a pall over the surrounding
country. An alarm was at once sent
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, OCTOBER 9, 1899.
branch County Jail, was dumped on the
operating table from the patrol wagon of
the Seventeenth street station. Davis,
who had been sentenced to six months
for malicious mischief, drew a razor
across his throat at 6 o'clock yesterday
morning. The slash reached from ear to
ear and required fifteen stitches of the
surgeon's needle to draw the cut together.
In his attempt to end his life he by the
barest possible shave drew the razor on
top of the windpipe and Jugular vein, but
did not penetrate deep enough to make
the job a success.
Sheriff Henry Martin's deputies at the
branch jail are very reticent as to how
Davis came to have a razor In his pos
session while locked up In his cell. It was
only a few weeks ago that five of the
prisoners obtained a dose of poison from
the drug store and came near fatally pois
oning themselves. It was through the
timely assistance of Dr. Crowley of the
County Hospital that they were saved.
Davis' case is another example of care
lessness on the part of the deputies In
charge of the branch Jail.
Dr. Woods Preaches.
Rev. Dr. E. A. Woods of the recently
consumed First Baptist Church preached
yesterday morning to his congregation at
the Y. M. C. A. Hall. His sermon was
on the subject "Do You Believe In Fate.""
and as a prelude to It he spoke a few
words on "Why We Honor Dewey." His
address on the great admiral was in brief
as follows:
"We honor Dewey because we find in
him a wonderful combination of noble ele
ments of character. Wise yet modest,
strict yet companionable, daring yet ten

flames from the Almshouse side.

The police believe that they have the
thief who stole a sack containing $4000
from the buggy of Captain John Barne
son on Market street last Saturday after
noon. The buggy was in charge of
James Barneson, a brother of the cap
tain. "He had drawn the money from the
bank and was on his way up Market
street, when he stopped to read The
Call's bulletins of the International
yacht race. While he was thus absorbed
the thief crawled Into the back of the
buggy and got away with the coin, which
was in a canvas sack.
Yesterday afternoon Police Officer
Rlordan of the Southern Station learned
that a man answering the description of
the thief was seen to enter a lodging
house at Sixth and Natoma streets.
Rlordan went to the house and closely
questioned the inmates. One of them,
whose name the police refuse to disclose
at the present time, claims that he met
a young man named James McFee as the
latter was walking upstairs of the lodg
ing-house. He had under his coat a sack
which he supposed was filled with coin.
He claims that he noticed the letters .
"U. S." stamped on the . sack. McFee
tried to avoid him, ami slipped into a
room on the second floor. As â¢ McFee
tallied with the description of the thief, [
Riordan lost no time in running him
down. He was found , In a saloon on j
Third street, and was at once taken to I
police headquarters. When questioned j
about the robbery he denied that he had
a hand in it, and declared that he would |
be able to establish his innocence at the |
proper time. Only a few dollars were :
found In his possession. McFee refused
to give nis address, but the police know !
that he was married a short time ago, j
and that he and his wife have been liv
ing In the vicinity of O'Farrell and De
visadero streets.
To-day Mrs. Drake, who saw the thief
get away with the sack of money, will
be taken to police headquarters for the
purpose of Identifying him. Chief Lees
is satisfied that he is the right man.
SECOND WEEK OF FESTIVAL.
The second week of the Youths' Direct
ory festival will open this evening. The |
purpose of the festival Is to clear the <
large debt which Is hanging over the Di
rectory, and It is deserving of support.
The success of this week's entertain
ment is almost assured before it begins, â 
owing to the number of societies which
have signified an Intention of lending a
helping hand. The programme Is also a
feature that will attract attention, and
will include numbers by some of the best
known professionals and amateurs of the ;
city.
To-night there will be a programme of j
vocal and instrumental music by the
League of the Cross band; also some club !
swinging by professionals. It will be St.
Anthony's booth night at home, and Miss
Belinda Roper, assisted by the following
ladies, will entertain their friends: Mes
dames Edward May, Hayes, Fox, Misses
Belinda Roper Minnie Harvey Fox, assist
ed by Mesdames Wade, Margaret
Schmidt, T. B. Hamilton. Martial Hain
que, Parolinl, Murfey, O'Doherty, Misses
Mary Roper, Annie E. Gavlgan, Frances
Slattery, Mary Harney, Carrie Gearon,
McGough. Lizzie O'Brien, Reynolds, Helen
Peck, Nellie Brady, Margaret Plunkett,
Kathleen Keating and J. J. Gildea.

Boston, has consented to be presnt and

There are various theories as to the
origin of tho blaze. Some argue that
the excessive heat of the day set the
dry leaves and underbrush ablaze.
Tramps make the place their rendez
vous, and it is possible that a spark
from one of their pipes might have
started the flames. The most prob
able solution Is the small boy with the
big shotgun who had been hunting
rabbits through the underbrush. A
piece of wadding from ' a shotgun
likely became Ignited and started the
Are. Shortly before the smoke was
noticed issuing from the canyon where
the fire started a small boy with a
gun was seen by Alfred Clancey of
329 Eureka street running from the
vicinity.
, The firemen suffered greatly from
the heat. During the progress of the
Are several attempts were made to
rob dwellings in the vicinity. The
Aames did not burn, themselves out
until 7 o'clock last evening.
JOE M'AULIFFE'S
BROTHER FRANK
SHOT TO DEATH
Frank Mitchell Fired
the Fatal Shot.
â¦ â
TROUBLE OVER AN ACCOUNT
m
BIG JOE BEFUSED TO PAY AND
A FIGHT ENSUED.
: â
Mitchell Armed Himself and There
Was a Second and a Fatal .
Struggle Says He Shot
in Self-Defense.
A quarrel over a small store account
ended In a shooting affair last night,
which cost Frank McAuliffe his life and
landed the shooter, Frank Mitchell, in
prison.
The affray occurred at the grocery store
and saloon of P. N. Flynn, corner of Ala
bama and Twenty-fourth streets, at 11
p. m. It appears that Mitchell, who keeps
a grocery and saloon on the corner of
Twenty-fourth and Harrison streets, had
a bill against big Joe McAuliffe, the ex
prize-lightet, who is now a deputy in the
County Clerk's office. He met Frank Mo-
Auntie a little after 11 o'clock and under
took to find out when Joe McAuliffe would
pay what he owed. McAuliffe asked how
much it was and was told that it amount
ed to about $10. Some remark made by
McAuliffe angered Mitchell and hot words
followed. Mitchell in an excited frame of
mind hurried away in the direction of his
store.
Bystanders say that Mitchell went away
to procure his pistol and that he returned
In a few minutes and walking up to with
in about ten feet of McAuliffe drew a 44
Smith & Wesson and without saying a
word fired, the bullet piercing McAuliffe
through the heart. The victim, died in
stantly.
Mitchell was detained and taken Into
custody later by Sergeant Blank and Po
liceman Pyron, who removed him to the
Seventeenth street station.
There Is another side to the story which
places Mitchell in a better light and ac
cording to Captain Glllen of the Seven
teenth street station gives the killing the
appearance of self-defense. Mitchell, at
a late hour this morning stated that he
met Frank and Joe McAuliffe on the
street and asked Joe when he was going
to pay the bill he owed him. He called
Joe s attention to the fact that he had a
good Job and was In a position to pay.
McAuliffe, applying an epithet to Mitchell,
said that he owed the bill but would
"never pay it." A scuffle ensued between
the three men and Mitchell went to his
place and returned shortly with the pis
tol. Another row was started, In which
Mitchell got the worst of it. He says he
shot McAuliffe when he was coming to
ward him and about four feet distant.
Mitchell has a badly cut and bruised
lip and chin to substantiate his state
ment and asserts that he was struck
first, was afraid of his life against the
two men and shot in self-defense.
The arresting officers reported that
they had a hard struggle with Joe Mc-
Auliffe after the arrest of Mitchell to
keep him from assaulting Mitchell.
Mitchell Is about 25 years of age. Mc-
Auliffe resided at Twentieth and Hamp
shire streets.
MAN WENT OVERBOARD
FROM THE BERKELEY
J. BENDER RESCUED AFTER A
COLD BATH.
Claims That He Was Jostled From
the Deck, but Police Say He
Took a Deliberate
Plunge.
J. Bender, a laborer residing at 307
Chattanooga s.treet in this city, was near
ly drowned about 6 o'clock last evening,
In the bay. off Goat Island. The fact that
he had some knowledge of swimming,
coupled with the prompt action of em
ployes on the ferry-boat Berkeley, pre
vented the man from covering a slab at
the Morgue.
Bender, with a party of friends, started
yesterday afternoon to make the return
trip across the bay on the ferry-boat
Berkeley. When the vessel neared Goat
Island a cry of "man overboard" went up
and It was found that Bender was strug
gling In the water. How he came to get
there Is still a question. He was under
the Influence of liquor and the police say
that he intentionally Jumped into the bay.
When Bender was brought to the Harbor
Hospital, where the superfluous water
was pumped from him, he made a claim
that some one had accidentally pushed
him overboard.
When Bender struck the water he ex
hibited some skill as a swimmer. Ha
managed, despite his condition, to keep
on the surface. In the meantime the
ferry-boat had been stopped and a boat
put off for the spot where the man's head
was seen boobing In the water. The boat
reached Bender just as he was about to
give up the struggle.
GARFIELD LEAGUE SOCIAL.
Preparing to Entertain Those Who
Assisted in the Monument
Exercises.
A meeting of the Garfield League was
held Saturday evening to arrange for an
entertainment, social and ball to be ten
dered the George H. Thomas and Lincoln
posts, G. A. R., and James A. Garfield
Corps, W. R. C. ; the League of the Cross
Cadets, Columbia 4 Park Boys' Club Asso
ciation. Veteran Guard and the various
organizations that participated in the
Garfield memorial exercises at Golden
Gate Park on the 7th tilt. The following
committee of arrangements was ap
pointed to prepare for the entertainment:
E. M. Galvin, Dawson Mayer, A. de la
Torre Jr., P. A. McGinness, H. L. Brad
ford. Joseph Meneses, T. F. Bachclder
E. A. Lorenzo, M. S. Blackburn, W. C.
Watson and Martin Murray.
It was decided to hold the entertain
ment on Saturday evening, October 21, at
8 o'clock, at the hall, 507 Sutter street,
corner of Powell. Persons who desire to
attend can procure complimentary tickets
from the secretary, room 4, 405 Montgom
ery street: Dawson Mayer, 000 Safe De
posit building, or A. de la Torre Jr., Oc
cidental Hotel.
Among the features of the entertain
ment will be a recitation by Major C. W.
Kyle, cornet solo by A. P. Black, songs
by Knickerbocker Quartet, song by Mrs.
Bert G. Adams, humorous remarks by
Charles Alpers, dialect stories and fancy
drill and tableau. There will be dancing
after the entertainment.
Disarming Chinese.
Officers doing police duty In Chinatown
are taking every precaution to avoid the
bloodshed which is usually attendant
upon the return of the Chinese fisher
men to this city. During .the past few
days hundreds of Chinese have been
stopped in the highways of Chinatown
and searched for weapons. This action is
taken to impress upon the hatchet wield
ers the vigilance of the police and their
determination to suppress any incipient
trouble. 'Â»â¢':Â»-&
As a rule when the hoards of fisher
men return to their winter homes more
or less trouble arises over the settlement
of . claims.

NANCE O'NEIL.
After a week of rollicking farce comedy,
the California gave Its patrons a legiti
mate classical comedy last night, the plot
of which lies In those good old days when
the tailor presented his bill each month
with as graceful and correct an obeisance
as the baronet was wont to do at court
receptions. Nance O'Neil and her excel
lent company of players know right well
the spirit of the play "Peg Wofflngton,"
and being evenly balanced they succeed
In getting all the quiet humor and intelli
gent wit therefrom which a clever author
has introduced. There is pathos, too,
which does not lack the necessary inter
pretation. Nance O'Neil has the make-up
of a great artist. "While she may lean
more toward legitimate tragedy, her fre
quent dives into the "element comlque"
are not at all unsuccessful. On the con
trary she receives liberal applause for
them. Next to Nance O'Neil, Barton Hill
is entitled to much of the credit for this
smooth performance. Here is an actor
who becomes younger the longer ne lives.
He is still as graceful as ever. The com
pany is far better than that of Frawley.
Miss Jessie Foster, a soprano who is
said to possess an unusually high range,
will give a song recital at Sherman-Clay
Hall to-morrow evening. Miss Foster,
having been very successful' East, should
not fail to attract a large audience of in
terested music lovers.
